The	  idea	  behind	  the	  measuring	  plan	  for	  the	  heating	  season	  was	  to	  ensure	  that	  even	  if	  one	  of	  the	  
weeks	  went	  wrong	  and	  the	  data	  was	  somehow	  unusable,	  the	  weeks	  before	  and	  after	  would	  still	  
be	  an	  on	  and	  an	  off	  week.	  This	  way	  the	  data	  would	  still	  be	  comparable	  with	  alternate	  on	  and	  off	  
weeks.	   The	   same	   could	   not	   be	   done	   for	   the	   non-­‐heating	   season	   due	   to	   many	   holidays	   in	   this	  
period,	  so	  instead	  two	  blocks	  each	  of	  three	  weeks	  with	  a	  pause	  week	  in	  between	  were	  planned	  
as	  shown	  in	  Table	  5-­‐1.	  
The	   experiments,	   which	   have	   been	   analysed,	   were	   carried	   out	   in	   the	   winter	   2014	   (heating	  
season)	  and	  in	  the	  spring	  2015	  (non-­‐heating	  season)	  in	  the	  same	  school	  year,	  so	  that	  the	  same	  
pupils	  have	  participated	  in	  both	  seasons.	  In	  all	  six	  weeks	  of	  the	  experiments	  they	  were	  allowed	  
to	  open	  and	  close	  the	  windows	  and	  doors	  in	  the	  classrooms	  as	  they	  wished.	  
As	  this	  project	  started	  after	  the	  first	  measurement	  period	  in	  winter	  2014	  ended,	  obviously	  these	  
measurements	  have	  not	  been	  a	  direct	  part	  of	  this	  project,	  but	  nevertheless	  the	  data	  is	  used	  to	  
include	  both	  heating	  and	  non-­‐heating	  conditions.	  However,	  due	  to	  the	  time	  limit	  of	  this	  project	  
and	  the	  second	  measuring	  period	  being	  carried	  out	  close	  to	  deadline,	  only	  performance	  tests	  and	  
questionnaires	  from	  the	  first	  three	  weeks	  in	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season	  2015	  have	  been	  analysed.	  
5.3.2. 	  CONTINUOUS	  MEASUREMENTS	  
During	   the	   six-­‐week	   experimental	   periods	   continuous	   measurements	   have	   been	   performed.	  
These	  include	  measurement	  of	  classroom	  temperature,	  relative	  humidity	  and	  CO2	  concentration.	  	  
The	  CO2	  concentration	  has	  been	  measured	  with	  a	  VAISALA	  GM20D	  sensor	  (accuracy:	  ±	  30	  ppm	  +	  
2	  %	  of	  reading)	  connected	  to	  a	  HOBO	  U12	  logger.	  A	  HOBO	  U12	  logger	  was	  used	  to	  log	  classroom	  
temperature	  and	  relative	  humidity	  (RH);	  see	  Figure	  5-­‐11.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐11:	  LEFT:	  HOBO	  U12	  LOGGER.	  RIGHT:	  VAISALA	  GM20D	  SENSOR.	  	  
Also,	  the	  outdoor	  temperature	  was	  measured	  with	  a	  Netatmo	  weather	  station	  (measuring	  range	  
outdoors:	  -­‐40	  °C	  to	  65	  °C,	  accuracy:	  ±	  0.3	  °C).	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:34==
	  
	  
	  
22	  
5.3.3. 	  SPOT	  MEASUREMENTS	  
Besides	  having	  loggers	  permanently	  logging	  different	  parameters	  during	  the	  six-­‐week	  periods,	  
tracer	  gas	  was	  used	  to	  get	  the	  air	  change	  rates	  in	  the	  classrooms,	  and	  this	  will	  be	  described	  in	  
the	  following.	  
AIR	  CHANGE	  RATE	  	  	  
The	  air	  change	  rates	  have	  been	  measured	  in	  the	  three	  classrooms	  to	  examine	  how	  they	  were	  
affected	  by	  the	  retrofits.	  
The	  air	  change	  rates	  were	  found	  using	  the	  decay	  method	  with	  tracer	  gas	  and	  the	  tracer	  gas	  used	  
was	  Freon	  134A.	  Freon	  was	  released	  in	  the	  classroom,	  the	  classroom	  room	  was	  left	  empty	  (the	  
cylinder	  with	  tracer	  gas	  removed)	  and	  the	  decay	  of	  Freon	  was	  followed	  using	  measurements	  
performed	  by	  the	  Innova	  1302	  Gas	  Monitor,	  see	  Figure	  5-­‐12.	  Two	  fans	  were	  placed	  inside	  the	  
classroom	  to	  ensure	  proper	  mixing	  of	  the	  tracer	  gas.	  The	  measurements	  were	  performed	  until	  
the	   decay	   curve	   followed	   the	   straight	   line	   on	   the	   semi-­‐log	   coordinate	   system.	   The	   decay	   was	  
followed	  in	  two	  locations	  in	  the	  occupied	  zone	  to	  check	  whether	  the	  air	  was	  properly	  mixed.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐12:	  INNOVA	  1302	  GAS	  MONITOR.	  
The	  air	  change	  rate	  was	  calculated	  by	  examining	  the	  rate	  of	  decay,	  as	  follows	  	  
	  
𝑛= ln 𝑐! −ln  (𝑐!)
𝑡! −𝑡!
	  
(1)	  
based	   on	   the	   measurements	   with	   the	   Innova	   1302	   Gas	   Monitor.	   The	   air	   change	   rate,	   n,	   was	  
calculated	  by	  means	  of	  the	  decay	  formula	  given	  by	  equation	  (1),	  where	  c1	  is	  the	  concentration	  at	  
the	  time	  t1,	  and	  c2	  is	  the	  concentration	  at	  the	  time	  t2.	  
Different	   scenarios	   were	   tested,	   including	   the	   two	   retrofits	   fully	   on	   and	   fully	   off;	   i.e.	   the	   air	  
change	  rate	  has	  been	  measured	  in	  all	  three	  classrooms	  with	  the	  windows	  fully	  open	  (and	  in	  the	  
classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  –	  the	  exhaust	  fan	  on	  100	  %)	  and	  fully	  closed	  (and	  in	  the	  classroom	  
with	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   –	   the	   exhaust	   fan	   off).	   Additionally,	   the	   air	   change	   rate	   was	   found	   for	   the	  
==PAGE:35==
	  
	  
	  
23	  
windows	  half	  opened	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  automatic	  window	  opening.	  These	  scenarios	  were	  
chosen	  in	  order	  to	  obtain	  the	  full	  range	  of	  possible	  air	  change	  rates	  for	  the	  different	  systems.	  	  
5.3.4. 	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  (HEATING	  SEASON	  2014)	  
Every	  week	  in	  the	  six-­‐week	  experimental	  period	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  2014,	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  
three	  classrooms	  have	  completed	  a	  subjective	  measure	  in	  terms	  of	  a	  paper	  questionnaire	  with	  
the	   following	   perceptions	   and	   symptoms:	   quality	   of	   classroom	   environment,	   thermal	  
environment,	  classroom	  air	  quality,	  air	  movement,	  acoustic	  environment	  of	  the	  classroom,	  air	  
humidity,	  illumination,	  ability	  to	  breathe,	  fatigue,	  headache,	  willingness	  to	  learn,	  dryness	  of	  lips,	  
skin	  and	  throat	  and	  general	  mood;	  see	  the	  questionnaire	  in	  Appendix	  III.	  To	  make	  it	  easy	  for	  the	  
pupils	  to	  fill	  in	  the	  questionnaire,	  the	  scale	  has	  been	  made	  as	  shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐13.	  It	  should	  be	  
mentioned	   that	   questions	   about	   acoustics	   and	   illumination	   have	   not	   been	   analysed	   in	   this	  
project.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐13:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  THE	  SCALE	  USED	  IN	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  IN	  THE	  HEATING	  SEASON.	  IT	  IS	  SCORED	  AS	  A=1,	  B=2,	  
C=3,	  D=4,	  E=5,	  F=6	  AND	  G=7.	  
5.3.5. 	  PERFORMANCE	  TESTS	  (HEATING	  SEASON	  2014)	  
Five	  different	  tests	  have	  been	  performed	  by	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  2014	  to	  find	  out	  
how	  the	  retrofits	  affected	  the	  performance	  of	  the	  pupils.	  These	  were	  subtraction,	  multiplication,	  
grammatical	  reasoning,	  reading	  speed	  and	  comprehension	  and	  d2-­‐test.	  The	  tests	  should	  be	  seen	  
as	   a	   natural	   part	   of	   the	   ordinary	   schoolwork,	   executed	   in	   appropriate	   lessons	   under	  
administration	  of	  their	  normal	  teacher,	  and	  have	  been	  developed	  to	  meet	  the	  academic	  level	  of	  
the	  pupils.	  The	  duration	  of	  the	  test	  should	  be	  short	  enough	  to	  make	  sure	  that	  the	  pupils	  would	  
not	  finish	  them	  in	  the	  time	  available.	  10	  minutes	  was	  set	  aside	  for	  each	  test,	  and	  if	  one	  pupil	  
finished	  the	  test	  before	  the	  10	  minutes,	  all	  pupils	  should	  stop,	  and	  the	  time	  should	  be	  accurately	  
noted.	  All	  tests	  will	  be	  explained	  in	  the	  following.	  
SUBTRACTION	  
The	  pupils	  have	  completed	  subtraction	  tests	  with	  columns	  of	  three	  and	  four	  digit	  numbers,	  in	  
which	  zero	  did	  not	  occur.	  The	  test	  result	  has	  been	  the	  number	  of	  subtractions	  attempted	  by	  each	  
pupil,	  leading	  to	  speed	  at	  which	  the	  test	  was	  completed,	  and	  the	  proportion	  of	  these	  in	  which	  the	  
answer	  was	  incorrect,	  leading	  to	  the	  percentage	  of	  error.	  An	  example	  of	  this	  test	  is	  shown	  in	  
Figure	  5-­‐14.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐14:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  SUBTRACTION	  TEST.	  	  
==PAGE:36==
	  
	  
	  
24	  
MULTIPLICATION	  
The	   pupils	   have	   completed	   multiplication	   tests,	   where	   two	   one-­‐,	   two-­‐	   or	   three-­‐digit	   numbers	  
should	   be	   multiplied.	   For	   4th	   grade	   the	   numbers	   were	   between	   1	   and	   12	   and	   for	   5th	  grade	  
between	  1	  and	  99,	  prepared	  in	  accordance	  with	  the	  math	  teachers.	  The	  test	  result	  has	  been	  the	  
number	   of	   multiplications	   attempted	   by	   each	   pupil,	   leading	   to	   speed	   at	   which	   the	   test	   was	  
completed,	   and	   the	   proportion	   of	   these	   in	   which	   the	   answer	   was	   incorrect,	   leading	   to	   the	  
percentage	  of	  error.	  An	  example	  of	  this	  test	  is	  shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐15.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐15:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  MULTIPLICATION	  TEST.	  
GRAMMATICAL	  REASONING	  
In	  this	  test	  the	  pupils	  were	  told	  to	  categorize	  statements	  as	  true	  or	  false.	  Statements	  could	  e.g.	  be	  
of	  the	  kind	  “A	  does	  not	  precede	  B	  in	  AB”	  (false)	  or	  “B	  is	  followed	  by	  A	  in	  BA”	  (true).	  The	  test	  
result	  has	  been	  the	  number	  of	  statements	  attempted	  by	  each	  pupil,	  leading	  to	  speed	  at	  which	  the	  
test	  was	  completed,	  and	  the	  proportion	  of	  these	  in	  which	  the	  answer	  was	  incorrect,	  leading	  to	  
the	   percentage	   of	   error.	   The	   results	   of	   this	   test	   have	   previously	   shown	   to	   be	   sensitive	   to	   the	  
surrounding	  environment	  (Baddeley	  2013).	  	  
READING	  SPEED	  AND	  COMPREHENSION	  
This	  test	  consists	  of	  a	  text	  of	  adequate	  level	  in	  which	  choice	  points	  have	  been	  inserted	  at	  regular	  
intervals.	  These	  choice	  points	  contain	  three	  words,	  which	  all	  fit	  into	  the	  sentence,	  but	  only	  one	  of	  
the	  words	  fit	  into	  the	  context.	  The	  test	  result	  has	  been	  the	  number	  of	  lines	  read,	  indicated	  by	  the	  
number	  of	  attempted	  choice	  points,	  leading	  to	  speed	  at	  which	  the	  test	  was	  completed,	  and	  the	  
proportion	  of	  these	  in	  which	  the	  answer	  was	  incorrect,	  leading	  to	  the	  percentage	  of	  error.	  An	  
example	  of	  this	  test	  is	  shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐16.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐16:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  READING	  AND	  COMPREHENSION	  TEST	  (IN	  DANISH).	  
This	  test	  was	  used	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  2014,	  but	  due	  to	  erroneous	  layout	  of	  the	  test,	  the	  results	  
could	  not	  be	  used	  in	  the	  analysis	  and	  have	  been	  eliminated.	  	  	  
D2-­‐TEST	  
The	  d2-­‐test	  is	  a	  standard	  test,	  which	  is	  used	  to	  measure	  pupils’	  ability	  to	  concentrate.	  The	  test	  
contains	  14	  rows	  of	  the	  letters	  d	  and	  p	  with	  one	  or	  two	  dashes	  above	  and/or	  below	  the	  letter.	  
The	   pupils	   were	   asked	   to	   mark	   as	   many	   of	   the	   so-­‐called	   target	   characters	   as	   possible	   for	   20	  
seconds	  per	  row.	  The	  target	  characters	  are	  all	  letters	  d	  with	  two	  dashes	  either	  above	  or	  below.	  
The	  result	  of	  this	  test	  has	  been	  the	  number	  of	  attempted	  characters,	  which	  indicates	  the	  speed	  at	  
which	  the	  test	  was	  performed,	  the	  number	  of	  errors	  indicating	  the	  accuracy	  of	  the	  performance	  
and	   the	   number	   of	   correctly	   marked	   target	   characters	   subtracted	   the	   number	   of	   incorrectly	  
==PAGE:37==
	  
	  
	  
25	  
marked	  distractor	  characters,	  indicating	  the	  overall	  concentration.	  An	  example	  of	  such	  a	  test	  is	  
shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐17.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐17:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  D2-­‐TEST.	  
This	   test	   has	   earlier	   shown	   to	   be	   a	   valid	   measure	   for	   speed	   and	   accuracy	   (Bates	   and	   Lemay	  
2004)	  and	  has	  also	  been	  used	  in	  another	  study	  about	  the	  effect	  of	  classroom	  indoor	  climate	  on	  
students’	  concentration	  (Twardella	  et	  al.	  2012).	  
5.3.6. 	  CHANGES	  FROM	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014	  TO	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015	  
The	   first	   test	   period	   in	   the	   winter	   2014	   was	   primarily	   used	   as	   a	   pilot	   project,	   to	   try	   out	   the	  
different	  methods.	  The	  measuring	  period	  in	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season	  has	  therefore	  included	  some	  
changes	  in	  the	  performance	  tests.	  The	  only	  test	  being	  the	  exact	  same	  as	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  
was	  the	  d2-­‐test,	  which	  was	  explained	  in	  section	  5.3.5.	  In	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season	  2015,	  the	  tests	  
were	   the	   d2-­‐test,	   a	   math	   test	   and	   a	   graphical/grammatical	   reasoning	   test,	   and	   the	   two	   last-­‐
mentioned	   will	   be	   explained	   in	   the	   following.	   Also,	   the	   layout	   of	   the	   questionnaire	   has	   been	  
changed	  with	  new	  phrasing	  of	  questions	  and	  new	  design	  of	  scales	  to	  make	  it	  easier	  for	  the	  pupils	  
to	  complete.	  The	  two	  new	  tests	  and	  the	  questionnaire	  have	  been	  designed	  by	  the	  test	  group	  in	  
accordance	  with	  the	  teachers.	  
MATH	  TEST	  
The	  subtraction	  and	  multiplication	  tests	  from	  earlier	  were	  combined	  into	  an	  overall	  math	  test.	  
The	  calculations	  were	  the	  same	  as	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  with	  numbers	  between	  1	  and	  12	  for	  4th	  
grade	  and	  between	  1	  and	  99	  for	  5th	  grade	  for	  multiplication	  and	  columns	  of	  three-­‐	  or	  four-­‐digit	  
numbers,	  in	  which	  zero	  did	  not	  occur	  for	  subtraction.	  The	  test	  contained	  alternating	  problems	  
with	  subtraction	  and	  multiplication,	  as	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐18.	  The	  test	  result	  has	  been	  the	  
number	   of	   calculations	   attempted	   by	   each	   pupil,	   leading	   to	   speed	   at	   which	   the	   test	   was	  
completed,	   and	   the	   proportion	   of	   these	   in	   which	   the	   answer	   was	   incorrect,	   leading	   to	   the	  
percentage	  of	  error.	  A	  time	  limit	  of	  8	  minutes	  was	  set	  for	  this	  test.	  	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐18:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  MATH	  TEST.	  
This	  change	  has	  been	  made	  to	  avoid	  that	  the	  pupils	  complete	  the	  test	  by	  routine,	  which	  might	  be	  
the	  case,	  if	  the	  test	  contains	  the	  same	  kind	  of	  problems	  all	  the	  way.	  Furthermore,	  it	  was	  desirable	  
to	  have	  fewer	  tests	  in	  the	  non-­‐heating	  period,	  accomplished	  by	  combining	  the	  two	  math	  tests	  in	  
the	  heating	  season	  into	  one	  overall	  math	  test.	  
	  
==PAGE:38==
	  
	  
	  
26	  
GRAPHICAL/GRAMMATICAL	  REASONING	  
This	  test	  has	  been	  designed	  to	  replace	  the	  grammatical	  reasoning	  test,	  as	  the	  new	  test	  seems	  
easier	  for	  pupils	  at	  the	  given	  class	  levels	  to	  complete.	  As	  for	  the	  old	  test	  of	  this	  kind,	  the	  pupils	  
should	   mark	   whether	   a	   sentence	   was	   true	   (S)	   or	   false	   (F).	   The	   sentences	   described	   a	   pair	   of	  
geometrical	   figures	   (circle/square/triangle).	   An	   example	   of	   this	   test	   is	   shown	   in	   Figure	   5-­‐19.	  
The	  test	  result	  was	  the	  number	  of	  sentences	  attempted	  by	  each	  pupil,	  leading	  to	  speed	  at	  which	  
the	  test	  was	  completed,	  and	  the	  proportion	  of	  these	  in	  which	  the	  answer	  was	  incorrect,	  leading	  
to	  the	  percentage	  of	  error.	  A	  time	  limit	  of	  5	  minutes	  was	  set	  for	  this	  test.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐19:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  GRAPHICAL/GRAMMATICAL	  REASONING	  TEST	  (IN	  DANISH).	  
QUESTIONNAIRE	  
Besides	  small	  changes	  in	  the	  phrasing	  of	  the	  questionnaires,	  the	  scales	  were	  changed	  to	  make	  it	  
easier	   for	   the	   pupils	   to	   complete;	   see	   the	   questionnaire	   for	   non-­‐heating	   season	   2015	   in	  
Appendix	  IV.	  The	  new	  scale	  is	  shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐20.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐20:	  NEW	  SCALE	  USED	  IN	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES.	  FROM	  LEFT	  THE	  SMILEYS	  ARE	  SCORED	  AS	  1,	  2.5,	  4,	  5.5	  AND	  7,	  
CORRESPONDING	  TO	  THE	  OLD	  SCALE.	  
5.3.7. 	  TEST	  PLAN	  
The	   test	   plan	   for	   the	   performance	   tests	   and	   questionnaires	   is	   shown	   in	   Table	   5-­‐2	   for	   both	  
heating	  season	  2014	  and	  non-­‐heating	  season	  2015.	  The	  test	  plan	  for	  the	  first	  test	  period	  was	  
made	  as	  a	  compromise	  between	  having	  several	  tests	  and	  at	  the	  same	  time	  not	  overloading	  the	  
pupils	  with	  too	  many	  test	  days.	  Fewer	  tests	  have	  been	  used	  in	  the	  second	  test	  period,	  as	  the	  idea	  
of	   this	   period	   was	   that	   the	   pupils	   should	   perform	   all	   tests	   in	   all	   six	   weeks	   to	   get	   more	  
representative	  results.	  This	  will	  assure	  that	  even	  though	  a	  test	  was	  not	  completed	  in	  one	  week,	  
there	  would	  still	  be	  two	  other	  weeks	  with	  the	  same	  condition	  giving	  sufficient	  data.	  
Weeks	   may	   occur,	   where	   the	   classes	   have	   not	   completed	   a	   given	   test	   or	   questionnaire,	   even	  
though	  it	  was	  on	  the	  plan.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:39==

5.3.8. 	  ENERGY	  USE	  
The	  heating	  and	  electricity	  consumption	  were	  measured	  continuously	  with	  meters	  installed	  in	  
the	  retrofitted	  classrooms.	  Heating	  energy	  was	  measured	  with	  a	  Kamstrup	  Multical	  602	  installed	  
on	  all	  radiators	  in	  the	  three	  classrooms	  –	  two	  in	  each	  classroom,	  (data	  sheet	  in	  Appendix	  V).	  The	  
electricity	  consumption	  was	  logged	  by	  an	  AAB	  electricity	  meter	  placed	  in	  the	  control	  room	  (data	  
sheet	  in	  Appendix	  VI).	  	  
5.3.9. 	  DATA	  PROCESSING	  AND	  STATISTICAL	  ANALYSIS	  
Excel	   for	   Mac	   2011	   version	   14.4.8	   was	   used	   for	   preliminary	   data	   processing,	   including	  
descriptive	  statistics,	  graphs	  and	  tables.	  
As	   the	   performance	   tests	   were	   examined,	   the	   total	   score	   was	   noted	   together	   with	   the	   total	  
number	  of	  correct	  answers	  and	  errors	  for	  each	  pupil	  and	  each	  test.	  The	  different	  performance	  
tests	  were	  analysed	  regarding	  the	  percentage	  of	  errors	  (PE)	  and	  speed	  at	  which	  the	  tests	  were	  
performed,	   as	   earlier	   studies	   have	   shown	   that	   these	   parameters	   are	   affected	   by	   the	   indoor	  
environment	   (Twardella	   et	   al.	   2012;	   Wargocki	   and	   Wyon	   2013).	   These	   factors	   have	   been	  
calculated	  with	  the	  following	  formulas	  (2)	  and	  (3).	  
	  
𝑃𝐸=
𝐸𝑟𝑟𝑜𝑟𝑠
𝑇𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙  𝑠𝑐𝑜𝑟𝑒∙100  %  [%]	  
(2)	  
	  
	  
𝑆𝑝𝑒𝑒𝑑= 𝑇𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙  𝑠𝑐𝑜𝑟𝑒
𝐷𝑢𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛                    [𝑎𝑡𝑡𝑒𝑚𝑝𝑡𝑠
𝑚𝑖𝑛.
]	  
	  
(3)	  
Many	   factors	   affect	   pupils’	   performance	   and	   perception	   of	   the	   indoor	   climate.	   A	   statistical	  
analysis	   can	   determine	   the	   probability	   that	   differences	   between	   the	   retrofits’	   on	   and	   off	  
conditions	  have	  occurred	  due	  to	  chance.	  The	  R	  software	  version	  3.1.2	  (R	  Core	  Team,	  2014)	  and	  
lme4	   (Bates	   et	   al.	   2014)	   was	   used	   to	   perform	   linear	   mixed	   model	   analysis	   (LMM)	   of	   the	  
relationship	  between	  the	  different	  retrofits	  and	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  and	  perception	  of	  the	  
==PAGE:40==
	  
	  
	  
28	  
indoor	   climate.	   Two	   tutorials	   were	   used	   as	   a	   help	   to	   perform	   the	   analysis	   (Winter	   2011a,	  
2011b).	  	  
LMM	  was	  used,	  as	  it	  takes	  into	  consideration	  the	  design	  of	  the	  study;	  the	  models	  are	  appropriate	  
for	  studies	  with	  repeated	  measures,	  where	  the	  dependent	  variable	  is	  measured	  more	  than	  once	  
for	  the	  same	  unit,	  and	  it	  can	  take	  into	  account	  the	  variation	  among	  individuals.	  Furthermore,	  the	  
independent	  variable	  may	  include	  both	  fixed	  and	  random	  effects	  (random	  factors	  sampled	  from	  
a	  larger	  sample	  space,	  such	  as	  the	  pupils	  within	  the	  classrooms).	  	  
In	  order	  to	  compare	  the	  different	  performance	  tests	  across	  the	  three	  systems,	  PE	  and	  speed	  have	  
been	  standardised	  with	  formulae	  (4).	  
	  
𝑥!"#$%#&%'()% =
𝑥−𝑚𝑖𝑛
𝑚𝑎𝑥−𝑚𝑖𝑛	  
	  
(4)	  
To	  check	  that	  differences	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  were	  not	  caused	  by	  other	  factors	  
than	  the	  retrofits,	  the	  reference	  classroom	  was	  analysed	  in	  the	  same	  way	  as	  the	  two	  classrooms	  
with	  retrofits.	  This	  means	  that	  whenever	  results	  from	  the	  two	  retrofit	  classrooms	  were	  split	  into	  
e.g.	   off	   weeks	   and	   on	   weeks,	   the	   same	   was	   done	   for	   the	   reference.	   Hence,	   the	   reference	  
conditions	  marked	  “off”	  are	  from	  Week	  1,	  Week	  4	  and	  Week	  5	  for	  the	  heating	  season	  and	  Week	  2	  
for	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season,	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  turned	  off	  in	  the	  retrofitted	  classrooms.	  The	  
reference	  conditions	  marked	  “on”	  are	  from	  Week	  2,	  Week	  3	  and	  Week	  6	  for	  the	  heating	  season	  
and	  from	  Week	  1	  and	  Week	  3	  for	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season,	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  turned	  on	  in	  
the	  retrofitted	  classrooms.	  
The	  statistical	  analysis	  was	  performed	  for	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  with	  percentage	  of	  error	  and	  
speed	  at	  which	  the	  tests	  were	  completed	  as	  dependent	  variables.	  The	  analysis	  was	  performed	  
for	  each	  class	  separately	  and	  with	  all	  tests	  included.	  The	  fixed	  effects	  were	  Condition	  (system	  
condition	   –	   either	   on	   (“on”)	   or	   off	   (“off”)	   and	   Test	   (type	   of	   test	   –	   grammatical	   reasoning	   /	  
multiplication	  /	  subtraction	  /	  d2	  /	  math	  /	  graphical/grammatical	  reasoning).	  Initials	  (the	  pupils’	  
initials,	  chosen	  by	  the	  test	  group)	  was	  a	  random	  effect;	  see	  Model	  A	  and	  B.	  In	  the	  model,	  ε	  is	  the	  
error	  term	  or	  residual	  –	  the	  random	  part	  of	  the	  model	  –,	  which	  stands	  for	  all	  the	  factors	  affecting	  
the	  dependent	  variable	  that	  cannot	  be	  explained	  by	  the	  model.	  	  
Interaction	  between	  Condition	  and	  Test	  was	  tested	  with	  the	  same	  model.	  The	  multiplication	  sign	  
indicates	  interaction.	  
	  
𝑃𝐸!"#$%#&%'()%  ~  𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛+   𝑇𝑒𝑠𝑡+ 𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛∗𝑇𝑒𝑠𝑡   +   1 𝐼𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙𝑠+   𝜀	  
	  
A 
	  
	   𝑆𝑝𝑒𝑒𝑑!"#$%#&%'()%  ~  𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛+ 𝑇𝑒𝑠𝑡+   𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛∗𝑇𝑒𝑠𝑡+   1 𝐼𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙𝑠+   𝜀	  
B 
	  
The	  analysis	  was	  performed	  separately	  for	  all	  tests	  and	  classrooms.	  Here,	  Condition	  was	  entered	  
as	  a	  fixed	  effect	  and	  Initials	  as	  a	  random	  effect,	  as	  shown	  in	  Model	  C	  and	  D.	  
	  
𝑃𝐸!"#$%#&%'()%  ~  𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛+ 1 𝐼𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙𝑠+   𝜀	  
	  
C 	  
	  
𝑆𝑝𝑒𝑒𝑑!"#$%#&%'()%  ~  𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛+ 1 𝐼𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙𝑠+   𝜀	  
	  
D 
	  
==PAGE:41==
	  
	  
	  
29	  
The	   questionnaires	   were	   also	   analysed	   statistically	   in	   R	   to	   determine	   the	   probability	   that	  
differences	   between	   the	   on	   (“on”)	   and	   off	   (“off”)	   conditions	   in	   perceptions	   and	   symptoms	  
occurred	   due	   to	   chance.	   The	   different	   perceptions	   and	   symptoms	   were	   analysed	   separately;	  
thermal,	   air	   quality,	   draught,	   stuffy	   nose,	   fatigue,	   headache,	   desire	   to	   work,	   lips,	   skin,	   throat,	  
wellbeing,	  eyes,	  power	  of	  concentration	  and	  sleepiness.	  Not	  all	  perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  were	  
analysed	   for	   all	   classes;	   only	   the	   four	   with	   the	   highest	   difference	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off	  
conditions	  were	  examined.	  
The	  three	  classes	  were	  analysed	  separately.	  The	  fixed	  effect	  was	  Condition	  (system	  condition	  –	  
either	   on	   or	   off),	   and	   the	   random	   effect	   was	   Initials	   (the	   pupils’	   initials,	   chosen	   by	   the	   test	  
group);	  see	  Model	  I	  below,	  where	  PerceptionX	  represents	  the	  different	  perceptions	  or	  symptoms	  
mentioned	  earlier.	  
	  
𝑃𝑒𝑟𝑐𝑒𝑝𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛!  ~  𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛+ 1 𝐼𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙𝑠+   𝜀	  
	  
E 
	  
For	  all	  analyses	  the	  assumptions	  were	  checked	  by	  visual	  inspection	  of	  residual	  plots,	  checked	  for	  
homoscedasticity,	  and	  in	  q-­‐q	  plots	  for	  normality.	  If	  the	  q-­‐q	  plot	  showed	  any	  clear	  deviation	  from	  
normality,	  a	  non-­‐parametric	  Wilcoxon	  signed-­‐rank	  test	  was	  applied.	  Examples	  of	  a	  residual	  plot	  
and	  a	  q-­‐q	  plot	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  5-­‐21	  and	  Figure	  5-­‐22.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐21:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  RESIDUAL	  PLOT,	  WHERE	  THE	  
HOMOSKEDASTICITY	  ASSSUMTPION	  IS	  MET.	  
	  
FIGURE	  5-­‐22:	  EXAMPLE	  OF	  Q-­‐Q	  PLOT,	  WHERE	  THE	  
NORMALITY	  ASSUMPTION	  SEEM	  TO	  WORK	  FINE.	  
Some	  deviation	  from	  the	  homoscedasticity	  assumption	  were	  seen,	  but	  as	  it	  can	  be	  difficult	  to	  
completely	  check	  if	  this	  assumption	  holds	  due	  to	  few	  observations,	  it	  has	  been	  assumed	  that	  this	  
model	  assumption	  held.	  Also,	  in	  some	  cases	  minor	  deviations	  from	  normality	  were	  found	  for	  the	  
residuals	  due	  to	  observations	  with	  very	  high	  or	  very	  low	  values,	  but	  it	  was	  assumed	  that	  the	  
model	  assumption	  regarding	  normality	  held	  in	  those	  situations.	  	  
When	  using	  the	  linear	  mixed	  model	  analysis,	  the	  P-­‐values	  were	  obtained	  by	  the	  likelihood	  ratio	  
test	  of	  the	  full	  model	  with	  the	  effect	  in	  question	  versus	  the	  model	  without	  the	  effect	  in	  question.	  
P-­‐values	  with	  the	  Wilcoxon	  test	  were	  found	  by	  a	  two-­‐tailed	  test,	  as	  a	  certain	  direction	  due	  to	  
change	  in	  the	  conditions	  from	  off	  to	  on	  was	  not	  expected.	  The	  relationship	  between	  the	  retrofits	  
==PAGE:42==
	  
	  
	  
30	  
and	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  and	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  was	  tested	  for	  significance	  at	  P	  
<	  0.05.	  	  
Appendix	  VII	  contains	  examples	  of	  the	  applied	  R	  scripts;	  one	  example	  for	  each	  model	  and	  one	  
example	  for	  the	  Wilcoxon	  analysis.	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:43==
	  
	  
	  
31	  
6. RESULTS	  
In	  this	  section	  the	  results	  from	  the	  test	  weeks	  are	  presented,	  starting	  with	  the	  results	  from	  the	  
heating	   season	   2014	   followed	   by	   the	   non-­‐heating	   season	   2015.	   Each	   of	   the	   two	   subsections	  
contains	   analyses	   of	   the	   indoor	   climate,	   including	   CO2	   concentration	   and	   temperature,	   the	  
pupils’	  performance	  and	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  environment.	  This	  is	  followed	  by	  a	  
subsection	   with	   the	   measured	   ventilation	   rates	   in	   the	   classrooms	   and	   the	   logged	   energy	  
consumption.	  Finally,	  the	  main	  results	  from	  the	  two	  seasons	  are	  summarized	  in	  a	  table.	  
Throughout	  the	  data	  processing,	  the	  three	  classes	  will	  be	  distinguished	  as	  follows:	  The	  reference	  
classroom	  will	  be	  red,	  the	  classroom	  with	  automatic	  window	  opening	  without	  the	  fan	  will	  be	  
green	  and	  the	  classroom	  with	  automatic	  window	  opening	  with	  the	  fan	  will	  be	  blue,	  whenever	  it	  
is	  possible.	  Furthermore,	  off	  weeks	  will	  be	  indicated	  by	  dashed	  lines,	  whenever	  possible.	  
6.1. 
HEATING	  SEASON	  2014	  
In	   the	   following,	   the	   results	   of	   the	   different	   measurements,	   performance	   tests	   and	  
questionnaires	  from	  the	  heating	  season	  2014	  (27th	  October	  2014	  to	  5th	  December	  2014)	  will	  be	  
presented.	  	  
6.1.1. 	  THE	  INDOOR	  ENVIRONMENT	  
The	   indoor	   environment,	   represented	   by	   temperature	   and	   CO2	   concentration,	   has	   been	  
examined	  in	  each	  of	  the	  three	  classrooms	  with	  HOBO	  loggers	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.2.	  In	  the	  
following,	  the	  data	  from	  these	  measurements	  will	  be	  presented.	  	  
CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  
Figure	  6-­‐1,	  Figure	  6-­‐2	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐3	  show	  CO2	  profiles	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom	  and	  the	  two	  
classrooms	  with	  the	  retrofits	  installed.	  The	  two	  weeks	  with	  lowest	  and	  highest	  maximum	  CO2	  
concentrations	  have	  been	  used	  as	  examples.	  Diagrams	  for	  all	  weeks	  and	  classes	  can	  be	  found	  in	  
Appendix	  VIII,	  in	  which	  also	  the	  temperature	  profiles	  are	  shown.	  The	  recommended	  limit	  for	  the	  
CO2	  concentration	  of	  1000	  ppm,	  according	  to	  section	  4.1,	  is	  shown	  as	  a	  black	  horizontal	  line	  in	  
the	  diagrams.	  	  
==PAGE:44==

33	  
	  
FIGURE	  6-­‐3:	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  DURING	  AN	  ON	  AND	  AN	  OFF	  WEEK	  IN	  THE	  CLASSROOM	  WITH	  THE	  AUTOMATIC	  WINDOW	  
OPENING	  AND	  THE	  FAN-­‐ASSISTED	  EXHAUST.	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	  
In	   all	   three	   classrooms	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   varied	   from	   day	   to	   day;	   some	   days	   had	   higher	  
peaks	  than	  others.	  
For	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  Figure	  6-­‐1,	  the	  CO2	  concentrations	  measured	  during	  the	  two	  weeks	  
were	  quite	  similar	  with	  concentrations	  up	  to	  about	  2600	  ppm,	  which	  exceed	  the	  recommended	  
level	   according	   to	   both	   the	   Danish	   Working	   Environment	   Authority	   and	   BR10.	   Only	   the	  
measurements	  on	  Friday	  differ	  by	  having	  a	  lower	  CO2	  concentration	  in	  Week	  5	  than	  in	  Week	  3.	  
Perhaps	   due	   to	   the	   pupils	   being	   more	   away	   from	   the	   classroom	   in	   Week	   5,	   or	   the	   pupils	   or	  
teacher	  opening	  the	  windows	  more	  often	  in	  Week	  5.	  	  
For	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits,	  the	  systems	  have	  a	  clear	  effect	  on	  the	  CO2	  concentration;	  
the	  peak	  values	  were	  lower	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  turned	  on.	  With	  the	  retrofits	  turned	  off,	  both	  
classrooms	  had	  CO2	  concentrations	  above	  3000	  ppm,	  which	  is	  also	  far	  above	  the	  recommended	  
level.	  From	  Figure	  6-­‐2	  it	  is	  seen	  that	  the	  AWO	  system	  reduced	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  to	  around	  
1500	  ppm,	  and	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  reduced	  the	  concentration	  to	  around	  1000	  ppm,	  as	  seen	  
in	  Figure	  6-­‐3.	  Moreover,	  the	  figures	  indicate	  that	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  almost	  always	  reached	  
the	  outdoor	  level	  before	  the	  next	  school	  day	  began.	  Even	  though	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  did	  not	  
reach	  the	  outdoor	  level	  some	  of	  the	  days,	  it	  did	  not	  affect	  the	  next	  day’s	  CO2	  concentration	  by	  
making	  it	  significantly	  higher.	  
In	   addition	   to	   the	   variation	   in	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   during	   a	   week,	   the	   weekly	   average,	  
minimum	   and	   maximum	   concentrations	   during	   the	   occupied	   hours	   are	   shown	   in	   Table	   6-­‐1.	  
Throughout	  the	  data	  processing,	  the	  occupied	  hours	  were	  assumed	  to	  follow	  the	  class	  lecture	  
schemes,	  if	  nothing	  else	  is	  mentioned;	  see	  these	  schemes	  in	  Appendix	  IX.

From	   Table	   6-­‐1	   it	   is	   seen	   that	   the	   average	   and	   maximum	   CO2	   concentration	   in	   the	   reference	  
classroom	  only	  varied	  a	  little	  between	  the	  weeks,	  having	  maximum	  levels	  around	  2500	  ppm	  –	  
slightly	  lower	  in	  week	  4	  and	  week	  5	  –,	  average	  values	  between	  1000	  ppm	  and	  1500	  ppm	  and	  
minimum	  levels	  around	  300	  ppm	  to	  400	  ppm,	  corresponding	  with	  the	  outdoor	  concentration.	  
The	  maximum	  level	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  was	  around	  500	  ppm	  lower	  than	  in	  the	  two	  other	  
classrooms	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  off.	  	  
The	  conditions	  in	  the	  off	  weeks	  were	  more	  or	  less	  the	  same	  for	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  
with	   similar	   average	   and	   maximum	   concentrations;	   still	   the	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   system	   provided	   a	  
relatively	  larger	  effect	  on	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  than	  the	  AWO	  system.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  
AWO,	  a	  slight	  decrease	  in	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  was	  seen	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system;	  both	  the	  
average	  and	  maximum	  levels	  were	  lower	  in	  the	  on	  weeks.	  Nevertheless,	  the	  average	  level	  in	  the	  
on	  weeks	  exceeded	  the	  recommended	  level	  of	  1000	  ppm	  according	  to	  BR10,	  with	  CO2	  levels	  as	  
high	  as	  2500	  ppm.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  the	  maximum	  CO2	  concentrations	  in	  the	  on	  
weeks	  were	  about	  half	  of	  the	  level	  in	  the	  off	  weeks,	  with	  maximum	  levels	  around	  3000	  ppm	  in	  
off	  weeks	  –	  slightly	  lower	  in	  week	  1	  –	  and	  around	  1200	  ppm	  in	  on	  weeks.	  When	  turning	  on	  the	  
system,	  the	  average	  concentration	  was	  below	  the	  recommended	  level	  of	  1000	  ppm	  according	  to	  
BR10.	  	  
Also,	  the	  standard	  deviation	  was	  smaller	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  on,	  indicating	  a	  more	  stable	  
CO2	  concentration	  in	  the	  on	  weeks	  with	  fewer	  high	  peaks,	  which	  was	  also	  seen	  from	  the	  CO2	  
profile	  plots	  –	  especially	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN.	  	  
==PAGE:47==
	  
	  
	  
35	  
Figure	   6-­‐4	   shows	   the	   cumulated	   percentage	   of	   occupied	   hours	   at	   or	   below	   a	   specific	   CO2	  
concentration.	  The	  acceptable	  (1000	  ppm)	  and	  maximum	  recommended	  (2000	  ppm)	  CO2	  levels	  
are	  shown	  as	  black	  vertical	  lines.	  
	  
FIGURE	  6-­‐4:	  CUMULATIVE	  GRAPH	  OF	  THE	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  DURING	  THE	  OCCUPIED	  HOURS	  IN	  THE	  THREE	  
CLASSROOMS,	  SEPERATED	  ACCORDING	  TO	  CONDITION.	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	  
In	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  was	  above	  1000	  ppm	  in	  approximately	  70	  %	  
of	  the	  occupied	  hours	  and	  above	  2000	  ppm	  in	  roughly	  10	  %	  of	  the	  occupied	  hours;	  this	  is	  seen	  to	  
be	  similar	  for	  both	  the	  “on”	  and	  the	  “off”	  weeks.	  This	  reference	  level	  is	  furthermore	  seen	  to	  be	  
quite	   similar	   for	   the	   two	   other	   classrooms	   with	   the	   systems	   turned	   off;	   perhaps	   the	   small	  
differences	  can	  be	  attributed	  to	  a	  different	  number	  of	  pupils	  present	  in	  the	  different	  classrooms.	  
Any	  differences	  in	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  in	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  are	  therefore	  likely	  
to	  be	  due	  to	  the	  systems.	  Turning	  on	  the	  AWO	  system	  in	  4a	  lowered	  the	  percentage	  of	  occupied	  
hours	  above	  1000	  ppm	  from	  about	  70	  %	  to	  about	  55	  %.	  Using	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  lowered	  
the	  percentage	  of	  occupied	  hours	  above	  1000	  ppm	  even	  more	  –	  from	  about	  70	  %	  to	  about	  20	  %.	  
Furthermore,	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  barely	  exceeded	  2000	  ppm	  with	  the	  AWO	  system	  turned	  on,	  
and	  never	  with	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  turned	  on,	  where	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  was	  always	  kept	  
below	  1500	  ppm.

As	  can	  be	  seen	  from	  the	  relatively	  low	  standard	  deviation,	  the	  temperature	  was	  rather	  stable	  
throughout	  all	  weeks	  in	  the	  occupied	  hours,	  and	  the	  results	  indicate	  that	  the	  temperatures	  in	  the	  
classes	  with	  the	  retrofits	  were	  slightly	  lower	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  on.	  With	  the	  systems	  on,	  
the	  temperatures	  varied	  between	  20.5	  °C	  and	  23.7	  °C	  for	  the	  classroom	  with	  the	  AWO	  system	  
and	   between	   20.4	   °C	   and	   22.6	   °C	   for	   5b	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN.	   For	   both	   systems	   the	   average	  
temperature	  decreased	  about	  1	  °C	  with	  the	  systems	  on,	  and	  also	  the	  peak	  temperatures	  were	  
reduced.	   For	   the	   reference	   classroom	   the	   same	   tendency	   with	   the	   lower	   temperatures	   in	   the	  
“on”	  weeks	  was	  seen,	  but	  less	  significant.	  Furthermore,	  a	  slightly	  higher	  standard	  deviation	  was	  
seen	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  indicating	  a	  more	  fluctuating	  temperature	  profile	  during	  the	  
occupied	  hours	  than	  in	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits.	  	  
The	  outdoor	  temperature,	  measured	  with	  Netatmo	  loggers,	  is	  shown	  in	  Table	  6-­‐3.	  The	  values	  
shown	  in	  the	  table	  are	  from	  8	  a.m.	  to	  3	  p.m.	  Monday	  to	  Friday.

6.1.2. 	  THE	  PUPILS’	  PERFORMANCE	  
In	  this	  section	  the	  results	  from	  the	  performance	  tests	  completed	  by	  the	  pupils	  will	  be	  presented.	  
For	   those	   tests,	   which	   have	   been	   performed	   in	   two	   weeks	   (multiplication)	   or	   four	   weeks	  
(subtraction,	   grammatical	   reasoning),	   only	   observations	   from	   pupils	   who	   participated	   in	  
minimum	  one	  off	  week	  and	  one	  on	  week	  were	  included	  in	  the	  analysis.	  The	  same	  procedure	  was	  
used	   for	   the	   reference	   classroom,	   so	   that	   only	   observations	   from	   pupils	   that	   participated	   in	  
minimum	  one	  week,	  aligned	  with	  the	  off	  weeks	  in	  the	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits,	  and	  minimum	  
one	  week,	  aligned	  with	  the	  on	  weeks	  in	  the	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits,	  were	  included.	  For	  the	  d2-­‐
test,	   which	   was	   performed	   in	   all	   six	   weeks,	   only	   observations	   from	   pupils	   participating	   in	   at	  
least	  two	  off	  weeks	  and	  two	  on	  weeks	  were	  included	  in	  the	  analysis;	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom	  
==PAGE:49==
	  
	  
	  
37	  
only	  observations	  from	  pupils	  who	  participated	  in	  minimum	  four	  weeks	  were	  included	  –	  two	  
aligned	  with	  the	  off	  weeks	  and	  two	  aligned	  with	  the	  on	  weeks	  in	  the	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits.	  	  
In	   Table	   6-­‐4	   the	   number	   of	   observations	   used	   for	   data	   processing	   is	   shown.	   For	   unknown	  
reasons	  the	  class	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  did	  not	  complete	  the	  subtraction	  test	  in	  week	  5,	  which	  was	  an	  
off	  week.

HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	  
Generally,	  the	  above	  graphs	  indicate	  only	  modest	  variations	  in	  PE	  and	  speed	  between	  the	  test	  
weeks,	   but	   the	   relatively	   large	   standard	   deviation	   suggests	   a	   large	   dispersion	   in	   the	   pupils’	  
responses	  within	  each	  class.	  	  
Figure	   6-­‐5	   and	   Figure	   6-­‐6	   show	   that	   PE	   for	   the	   grammatical	   reasoning	   test	   in	   the	   reference	  
classroom	   was	   more	   or	   less	   constant	   during	   the	   test	   weeks,	   though	   increasing	   somewhat	  
between	  week	  1	  and	  2.	  The	  pupils	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  seemed	  to	  work	  slower	  from	  week	  
==PAGE:52==
	  
	  
	  
40	  
1	  to	  2	  and	  from	  week	  5	  to	  6.	  For	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  PE	  was	  almost	  constant	  between	  
week	  1	  and	  2,	  and	  decreased	  from	  week	  5	  to	  6,	  whereas	  the	  pupils’	  speed	  was	  constant	  in	  all	  
four	  weeks.	  Overall,	  the	  decrease	  in	  PE	  from	  week	  1	  to	  6	  might	  be	  an	  indication	  of	  the	  learning	  
effect.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  the	  general	  tendency	  was	  that	  the	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  
errors	   with	   the	   system	   turned	   on	   –	   PE	   decreased	   between	   week	   1	   and	   2	   and	   again	   between	  
week	  5	  and	  6.	  With	  regard	  to	  speed	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  worked	  more	  
or	  less	  at	  the	  same	  pace	  in	  week	  1,	  2	  and	  6,	  whereas	  a	  slightly	  higher	  speed	  was	  observed	  in	  
week	  5.	  
For	  multiplication,	  Figure	  6-­‐7	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐8	  show	  that	  there	  was	  only	  a	  small	  variation	  in	  both	  
PE	  and	  speed	  for	  this	  test	  –	  maybe	  due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  this	  test	  was	  only	  performed	  during	  two	  
weeks.	   Though,	   there	   was	   a	   small	   tendency	   that	   the	   pupils	   in	   the	   reference	   classroom	   made	  
fewer	  errors	  in	  week	  4,	  whereas	  both	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  made	  fewer	  errors	  in	  week	  3,	  
being	  the	  on	  week.	  	  
Figure	  6-­‐9	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐10	  show	  no	  significant	  variation	  in	  PE	  for	  either	  the	  reference	  or	  the	  
classroom	   with	   AWO	   for	   the	   subtraction	   test.	   The	   pupils	   in	   the	   reference	   classroom	   worked	  
notably	  faster	  in	  week	  6	  than	  in	  week	  5,	  whereas	  no	  change	  in	  speed	  was	  seen	  between	  week	  1	  
and	  2.	  The	  fact	  that	  the	  speed	  increased	  overall	  week	  1	  to	  6	  as	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐10	  might	  be	  an	  
indication	  of	  the	  learning	  effect.	  Due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  no	  subtraction	  test	  was	  performed	  in	  week	  5	  
in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  it	  can	  be	  difficult	  to	  clearly	  identify	  tendencies	  over	  the	  four	  
weeks,	  but	  the	  result	  indicates	  that	  the	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  errors	  and	  worked	  slower	  in	  the	  on	  
weeks	  than	  in	  the	  off	  week.	  
Finally,	  the	  weekly	  average	  of	  PE	  and	  speed	  for	  the	  d2-­‐test	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐11	  and	  Figure	  
6-­‐12.	  For	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  PE	  increased	  over	  the	  first	  five	  weeks,	  after	  which	  a	  drop	  was	  
seen	  from	  week	  5	  to	  6.	  The	  speed	  was	  quite	  constant	  over	  the	  first	  four	  weeks,	  followed	  by	  an	  
increase	  from	  week	  4	  to	  6.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  PE	  increased	  from	  week	  1	  to	  2	  –	  so	  more	  
errors	  were	  made	  in	  the	  on	  week	  –	  after	  which	  it	  decreased	  until	  week	  5.	  The	  last	  week,	  being	  an	  
on	  week,	  the	  pupils	  made	  significantly	  more	  errors	  –	  perhaps	  because	  the	  pupils	  were	  tired	  of	  
continuously	  doing	  the	  same	  test.	  Regarding	  speed,	  there	  was	  a	  decrease	  from	  week	  1	  to	  3,	  after	  
which	  the	  speed	  was	  almost	  linearly	  increasing	  until	  week	  6	  –	  perhaps	  indicating	  the	  learning	  
effect.	   In	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN,	   PE	   was	   almost	   constant	   throughout	   the	   six	   weeks,	  
whereas	  the	  speed	  showed	  some	  variation	  –	  especially	  during	  the	  first	  three	  weeks,	  where	  the	  
speed	  first	  decreased	  from	  week	  1	  to	  2	  and	  then	  increased	  from	  week	  2	  to	  3.	  
The	  differences	  in	  pupil	  performance	  between	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  have	  been	  further	  analysed.	  
For	  that	  purpose,	  hypotheses	  for	  PE	  and	  speed	  were	  set	  up:	  
• 
PE	  is	  reduced	  when	  the	  system	  is	  on	  
• 
Speed	  increases	  when	  the	  system	  is	  on	  
For	  each	  pupil	  the	  difference	  in	  performance	  between	  on	  and	  off	  weeks	  was	  calculated.	  These	  
differences	   were	   averaged	   and	   their	   standard	   deviation	   was	   calculated.	   The	   difference	   in	  
standardised	   PE	   was	   calculated	   as	   the	   average	   of	   the	   values	   in	   the	   on	   condition	   minus	   the	  
average	  of	  the	  PE	  in	  the	  off	  condition;	  this	  yields	  a	  negative	  result	  if	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  mistakes	  
when	   the	   system	   was	   on.	   Similar	   calculation	   was	   made	   for	   the	   standardised	   speed,	   with	   the	  
==PAGE:53==
	  
	  
	  
41	  
average	  of	  the	  values	  in	  the	  on	  condition	  minus	  the	  average	  of	  the	  values	  in	  the	  off	  condition;	  this	  
yields	  a	  positive	  result	  if	  pupils	  worked	  faster	  when	  the	  system	  was	  on.	  	  
Figure	   6-­‐13	   shows	   the	   differences	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off	   conditions	   for	   PE	   and	   speed	   as	   an	  
average	  of	  the	  results	  from	  all	  tests,	  including	  grammatical	  reasoning,	  multiplication,	  subtraction	  
and	   d2-­‐test.	   The	   P-­‐values	   were	   found	   as	   described	   in	   section	   5.3.9	   with	   Model	   A	   (PE)	   and	   B	  
(speed),	  testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  6-­‐13:	  DIFFERENCES	  BETWEEN	  ON	  AND	  OFF	  CONDITIONS	  FOR	  PE	  AND	  SPEED	  FOR	  THE	  TWO	  SYSTEMS	  WITH	  ALL	  
TESTS	  INCLUDED.	  THE	  VALUES	  ARE	  GIVEN	  AS	  THE	  DIFFERENCE	  IN	  MEAN	  +/-­‐	  S.D.	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	  
From	  Figure	  6-­‐13	  it	  is	  seen	  that	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  the	  pupils	  made	  significantly	  
less	  errors	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on	  (P	  =	  0.008),	  but	  there	  was	  no	  effect	  on	  the	  speed.	  In	  the	  
classroom	  with	  AWO,	  the	  pupils	  worked	  significantly	  slower	  (P	  <	  0.001)	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  
on,	   with	   no	   effect	   on	   errors.	   For	   the	   reference	   classroom	   no	   significant	   change	   was	   seen	   for	  
either	  PE	  or	  speed.	  
The	  analysis	  was	  repeated	  with	  the	  results	  for	  PE	  and	  speed	  separately	  for	  the	  four	  tests.	  The	  
results	  of	  this	  analysis	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐14	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐15.	  The	  P-­‐values	  were	  found	  as	  
described	  in	  section	  5.3.9	  with	  Model	  C	  (PE)	  and	  D	  (speed),	  testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  A	  
negative	  result	  indicates	  fewer	  PE	  or	  lower	  speed	  when	  on	  (“on”).

43	  
According	  to	  Figure	  6-­‐14	  there	  was	  a	  tendency	  towards	  the	  pupils	  making	  fewer	  errors	  in	  the	  
majority	  of	  the	  tests	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  on.	  Especially,	  there	  seems	  to	  be	  an	  effect	  on	  PE	  in	  
the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN;	  with	  the	  system	  running	  the	  pupils	  made	  significantly	  fewer	  
errors	   in	   both	   the	   grammatical	   reasoning	   test	   (P	   =	   0.002),	   which	   was	   also	   clearly	   seen	   from	  
Figure	   6-­‐5,	   and	   the	   subtraction	   tests	   (P	   =	   0.007),	   and	   they	   also	   made	   fewer	   errors	   in	   the	  
multiplication	   test	   –	   though	   not	   significant.	   The	   large	   difference	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off	  
conditions	  for	  the	  subtraction	  test	  might	  to	  some	  extent	  be	  due	  to	  the	  missing	  week	  5,	  being	  an	  
off	  week.	  The	  opposite	  was	  the	  case	  for	  the	  d2-­‐test,	  where	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  
made	   significantly	   more	   errors,	   with	   the	   system	   turned	   on	   (P	   <	   0.001)	   –	   perhaps	   due	   to	   the	  
relatively	  higher	  PE	  in	  week	  6.	  In	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  no	  significant	  difference	  between	  “on”	  
and	  “off”	  condition	  for	  PE	  was	  seen.	  	  
Regarding	  speed,	  Figure	  6-­‐15,	  the	  results	  indicate	  a	  systematic	  effect	  on	  speed	  in	  the	  retrofit	  
classrooms;	  the	  pupils	  were	  seen	  to	  work	  slower	  with	  the	  retrofits	  in	  operation.	  The	  pupils	  in	  
the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  worked	  significantly	  slower	  in	  three	  out	  of	  four	  tests	  –	  grammatical	  
reasoning	   (P	   =	   0.01),	   subtraction	   (P	   <	   0.001)	   and	   d2-­‐test	   (P	   =	   0.04).	   Also	   the	   pupils	   in	   the	  
classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   worked	   slower	   in	   all	   four	   tests,	   when	   the	   system	   was	   running	   –	  
though	  not	  significant.	  The	  pupils	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  worked	  significantly	  slower	  in	  the	  
grammatical	   reasoning	   test	   (P	   =	   0.002)	   in	   the	   weeks,	   which	   were	   on	   weeks	   for	   the	   two	  
classrooms	  with	  retrofits,	  whereas	  they	  were	  seen	  to	  work	  faster	  in	  the	  “on”	  weeks	  in	  the	  three	  
other	  tests.	  
As	   different	   tendencies	   for	   the	   four	   tests	   were	   seen,	   it	   is	   likely	   that	   the	   effect	   of	   condition	  
depended	  on	  the	  test	  type.	  Therefore	  the	  interaction	  between	  the	  test	  type	  and	  the	  condition	  
(on/off)	   was	   analysed	   with	   the	   linear	   mixed	   model	   analysis,	   Model	   A	   and	   B,	   as	   described	   in	  
section	  5.3.9,	  and	  the	  results	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐16	  to	  Figure	  6-­‐21.

For	   the	   reference	   classroom,	   Figure	   6-­‐16	   and	   Figure	   6-­‐17,	   no	   interaction	   was	   seen	   for	   PE	  
between	  condition	  and	  test,	  whereas	  there	  was	  a	  tendency	  towards	  interaction	  for	  speed	  (P	  =	  
0.05).	  Both	  PE	  and	  speed	  depended	  on	  test	  type	  (P	  =	  0.04	  for	  PE	  and	  P	  <	  0.001	  for	  speed).	  Figure	  
6-­‐18	  suggests	  that	  there	  was	  significant	  interaction	  between	  condition	  and	  test	  type	  for	  PE	  in	  
the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  (P	  =	  0.01).	  With	  a	  significance	  level	  of	  P	  =	  0.06,	  some	  interaction	  was	  
likewise	  seen	  between	  condition	  and	  test	  type	  for	  speed	  in	  the	  same	  classroom,	  according	  to

Figure	   6-­‐19.	   It	   was	   further	   found	   that	   the	   effect	   of	   test	   type	   on	   PE	   was	   significant	   in	   the	  
classroom	  with	  AWO	  (P	  <	  0.001),	  whereas	  there	  was	  a	  significant	  effect	  of	  both	  test	  type	  (P	  =	  
0.02)	   and	   condition	   (P	   <	   0.001)	   on	   speed.	   According	   to	   Figure	   6-­‐20	   and	   Figure	   6-­‐21	   no	  
interaction	   was	   seen	   between	   test	   type	   and	   condition	   in	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   for	  
either	  PE	  (P	  =	  0.28)	  or	  speed	  (P	  =	  0.72).	  However,	  it	  seems	  like	  test	  type	  had	  the	  main	  influence	  
on	  speed	  (P	  <	  0.001).	  For	  PE,	  on	  the	  contrary,	  there	  seemed	  to	  be	  a	  significant	  effect	  of	  both	  
condition	  (P	  =	  0.008)	  and	  test	  type	  (P	  <	  0.001).	  	  
After	  closer	  examination	  of	  the	  results	  from	  the	  test	  of	  interaction,	  no	  systematic	  patterns	  were	  
found,	  and	  the	  mechanisms,	  which	  caused	  this	  interaction,	  were	  not	  detected.	  
6.1.3. 	  THE	  PUPILS’	  PERCEPTION	  OF	  THE	  INDOOR	  CLIMATE	  AND	  THEIR	  SYMPTOMS	  
To	   evaluate	   the	   pupils’	   perception	   of	   the	   indoor	   climate,	   they	   were	   asked	   to	   complete	   a	  
questionnaire	  in	  each	  of	  the	  weeks	  in	  the	  measuring	  period.	  For	  unknown	  reasons,	  there	  have	  
been	  no	  data	  from	  test	  week	  2,	  being	  an	  on	  week,	  for	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN.	  
The	  same	  inclusion	  criterion	  was	  used	  for	  responses	  of	  the	  questionnaires	  as	  in	  the	  case	  of	  the	  
performance	  tests.	  In	  Table	  6-­‐5	  the	  number	  of	  responses,	  which	  was	  used	  in	  the	  analysis	  of	  the	  
questionnaire	  data,	  is	  shown	  for	  the	  three	  classes.	  
TABLE	  6-­‐5:	  NUMBER	  OF	  OBSERVATIONS	  USED	  FOR	  ANALYSIS	  OF	  QUESTIONNAIRE	  DATA.	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.

Figure	  6-­‐22,	  Figure	  6-­‐23	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐24	  show	  the	  results	  from	  the	  questionnaires	  completed	  by	  
the	   pupils	   during	   the	   six-­‐week	   period.	   The	   results	   are	   shown	   as	   weekly	   mean	   values	   of	   the	  
perceptions	   and	   symptoms.	   The	   scale	   ranged	   from	   1	   to	   7	   as	   defined	   in	   Figure	   5-­‐13;	   for	  
perception	  1	  =	  very	  satisfied	  and	  for	  symptoms	  1	  =	  low	  intensity.	  Tables	  with	  mean	  values	  and	  
standard	  deviations	  for	  the	  different	  responses	  on	  the	  questionnaires	  can	  be	  found	  in	  Appendix	  
X.	  It	  should	  be	  noted	  that	  here	  the	  different	  colours	  do	  not	  represent	  the	  different	  classrooms,	  
but	  instead	  they	  represent	  each	  of	  the	  six	  test	  weeks.

FIGURE	  6-­‐24:	  PERCEPTIONS	  AND	  SYMPTOMS	  FROM	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  COMPLETED	  BY	  THE	  PUPILS	  IN	  THE	  
CLASSROOM	  WITH	  AUTOMATIC	  WINDOW	  OPENING	  WITH	  FAN-­‐ASSISTED	  EXHAUST.	  	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	  THERE	  WAS	  
NO	  DATA	  FROM	  WEEK	  2.	  
For	  the	  reference	  classroom	  and	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  there	  were	  no	  very	  large	  differences	  
in	  the	  pupils’	  perceptions	  or	  symptoms	  between	  the	  test	  weeks.	  However,	  some	  perceptions	  and	  
symptoms	   stand	   out	   by	   having	   larger	   variations	   during	   the	   six	   weeks	   than	   others.	   For	   the	  
reference	  room	  the	  largest	  differences	  were	  seen	  for	  the	  symptoms	  lips,	  skin,	  throat	  and	  eyes.	  A	  
reason	  for	  the	  variation	  might	  be	  that	  it	  perhaps	  was	  difficult	  for	  the	  pupils	  to	  make	  up	  their	  
mind	  about	  those	  symptoms.	  For	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  the	  symptoms	  stuffy	  nose	  and	  lips	  in	  
week	  6	  were	  worse	  and	  a	  worsening	  in	  the	  perception	  of	  draught	  in	  week	  3	  –	  both	  weeks	  with	  
the	  AWO	  system	  turned	  on.	  	  
For	   the	   classroom	   with	   the	   AWO	   +	   FAN,	   there	   was	   a	   larger	   difference	   in	   perception	   and	  
symptoms	   from	   off	   to	   on	   condition	   than	   for	   the	   other	   two	   classrooms.	   Especially,	   an	  
improvement	   in	   the	   pupils’	   perception	   of	   the	   air	   quality	   and	   the	   temperature	   was	   seen.	  
Furthermore,	  it	  is	  noted	  that	  the	  pupils	  were	  generally	  satisfied	  with	  the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  had	  
few	  symptoms	  in	  week	  1,	  even	  though	  it	  was	  an	  off	  week.	  	  
To	  be	  able	  to	  clearly	  see	  any	  changes	  in	  symptoms	  and	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  between	  
the	   three	   classrooms,	   the	   diagram	   has	   been	   made	   with	   both	   systems	   and	   the	   reference	  
classroom	   included,	   distinguishing	   between	   on	   and	   off	  conditions;	   see	   Figure	   6-­‐25.	   Here,	   the	  
different	  colours	  represent	  each	  classroom.

FIGURE	  6-­‐25:	  PERCEPTIONS	  AND	  SYMPTOMS	  FROM	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  COMPLETED	  BY	  THE	  PUPILS	  IN	  THE	  
CLASSROOMS	  WITH	  THE	  DIFFERENT	  SYSTEMS	  TURNED	  OFF	  AND	  ON	  AND	  IN	  THE	  REFERENCE	  CLASSROOM.	  HEATING	  
SEASON	  2014.	  
From	  this	  diagram	  it	  can	  be	  seen	  that	  there	  were	  only	  modest	  differences	  between	  the	  on	  and	  
the	   off	   condition	   for	   the	   two	   systems.	   To	   visualize	   the	   difference	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off	  
conditions	  for	  the	  two	  systems,	  the	  difference	  between	  on	  and	  off	  weeks	  was	  calculated	  for	  each	  
pupil	  and	  these	  differences	  were	  averaged.	  The	  difference	  in	  score	  between	  on	  and	  off	  weeks	  
was	  calculated	  from	  equation	  (5).	  
	  
𝐷𝑖𝑓𝑓𝑒𝑟𝑒𝑛𝑐𝑒!"/!"" = 𝑂𝑓𝑓−𝑂𝑛	  
	  
(5)	  
As	  a	  lower	  score	  indicates	  more	  satisfied	  pupils,	  a	  positive	  result	  of	  equation	  (5)	  indicates	  an	  
improvement	   in	   the	   pupils’	   perception	   of	   the	   indoor	   climate	   and	   their	   symptoms	   in	   the	   on	  
condition.	  Figure	  6-­‐26	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐27	  show	  the	  results	  for	  the	  two	  systems.	  The	  P-­‐values	  are	  
found	  with	  Model	  E,	  testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition,	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.9.	  	  
Only	  the	  significance	  levels	  for	  the	  four	  major	  differences	  have	  been	  found;	  that	  is	  temperature,	  
stuffy	  nose,	  eyes	  and	  desire	  to	  work	  for	  AWO	  and	  temperature,	  air	  quality,	  lips	  and	  throat	  for	  
AWO	   +	   FAN.	   The	   differences	   between	   ”off”	   and	   “on”	   condition	   for	   the	   reference	   are	   likewise	  
shown	  in	  both	  diagrams.

Figure	  6-­‐26	  shows	  that	  the	  differences	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  were	  generally	  low	  in	  
the	   classroom	   with	   AWO,	   so	   even	   though	   there	   seemed	   to	   be	   some	   effect	   on	   the	   CO2	  
concentration,	  it	  might	  have	  been	  too	  small	  for	  the	  pupils	  to	  feel	  it.	  The	  only	  parameter	  standing	  
out	  was	  the	  stuffy	  nose	  symptom,	  which	  got	  significantly	  worse,	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system	  (P	  =	  
0.002).	   No	   significant	   change	   in	   this	   symptom	   was	   seen	   in	   the	   reference	   classroom.	   The	  
symptom	  eyes	  also	  got	  worse,	  indicating	  that	  the	  pupils	  felt	  drier	  eyes	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  
on;	  the	  same	  was	  seen	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  in	  which	  there	  was	  a	  significant	  worsening	  in	  
the	  eye	  symptom	  (P	  =	  0.02).	  Furthermore,	  the	  pupils	  felt	  an	  increased	  desire	  to	  work	  when	  the	  
AWO	  system	  was	  on,	  which	  was	  likewise	  seen	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom.	  Finally,	  there	  was	  a	  
worsening	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  temperature,	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system,	  which	  was	  
not	  seen	  to	  the	  same	  extent	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom.	  
Figure	  6-­‐27	  shows	  that	  there	  were	  generally	  small	  differences	  between	  on	  and	  off	  condition	  for	  
most	  of	  the	  perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN.	  Though	  there	  was	  a	  
significant	  improvement	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  air	  quality	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on	  (P	  
<	  0.001).	  The	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  air	  quality	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  did	  not	  change,	  
indicating	   that	   the	   change	   seen	   in	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   was	   probably	   due	   to	   the	  
system.	  Furthermore,	  the	  perception	  of	  the	  temperature	  improved,	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system,	  
and	  a	  worsening	  in	  the	  symptoms	  lips	  and	  throat	  was	  seen,	  indicating	  that	  the	  pupils	  felt	  drier	  
lips	  and	  throat	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on.	  Neither	  of	  these	  tendencies	  was	  seen	  in	  the	  reference	  
classroom.	  
6.1.4. 	  SUMMARY	  OF	  THE	  P-­‐VALUES	  FOUND	  FROM	  THE	  STATISTICAL	  ANALYSIS	  OF	  THE	  
SYSTEMS’	  EFFECTS	  ON	  THE	  PUPILS’	  PERFORMANCE	  AND	  PERCEPTIONS,	  HEATING	  
SEASON	  2014	  
In	   the	   following,	   all	   results	   from	   the	   statistical	   analysis	   of	   the	   systems’	   effect	   on	   pupils’	  
performance	   and	   perceptions	   are	   summarized.	   Also	   the	   applied	   test	   method	   is	   mentioned	   –	  
either	  the	  linear	  mixed	  model	  analysis	  (LMM)	  or	  the	  Wilcoxon	  test.	  
PERFORMANCE	  
Table	   6-­‐6	   summarizes	   the	   results	   from	   the	   analysis	   with	   one	   system	   and	   all	   tests	   included;	  
Model	  A	  and	  B,	  explained	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  were	  used	  when	  analysing	  the	  effects	  of	  Condition	  and	  
Test	  and	  the	  interaction	  between	  the	  two.	  Table	  6-­‐7	  presents	  the	  results	  from	  the	  analysis	  with	  
each	  test	  and	  each	  system	  separately;	  Model	  C	  and	  D,	  presented	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  were	  applied	  to	  
test	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  The	  effects	  marked	  with	  (#)	  are	  those,	  which	  were	  borderline	  
significant,	  meaning	  that	  there	  might	  be	  a	  trend	  towards	  significance.

differences	   between	   on	   and	   off	  condition	   have	   been	   estimated	   with	   Model	   E,	   as	   explained	   in	  
section	  5.3.9,	  testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  The	  results	  are	  summarized	  in	  Table	  6-­‐8.

6.2. 
NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015	  	  
In	  the	  following,	  the	  data	  from	  the	  measurements,	  performance	  tests	  and	  questionnaires	  from	  
the	   non-­‐heating	   season	   2015	   will	   be	   analysed.	   Due	   to	   the	   time	   limit	   of	   this	   project,	   only	   the	  
graphical/grammatical	  reasoning	  and	  math	  tests	  and	  the	  questionnaires	  from	  the	  first	  three	  test	  
weeks	  have	  been	  analysed;	  from	  20th	  of	  April	  2015	  to	  8th	  of	  May	  2015.	  	  
The	  data	  processing	  follows	  the	  same	  structure	  as	  for	  the	  heating	  season,	  so	  some	  description	  
has	  been	  omitted	  in	  this	  section.	  	  
6.2.1. 	  THE	  INDOOR	  ENVIRONMENT	  
The	  indoor	  environment,	  represented	  by	  temperature	  and	  CO2	  concentration,	  was	  examined	  in	  
each	  of	  the	  three	  classrooms	  with	  HOBO	  loggers	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.2.	  In	  the	  following,	  
the	  data	  from	  these	  measurements	  will	  be	  described.	  	  
CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  
Figure	  6-­‐28,	  Figure	  6-­‐29	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐30	  show	  CO2	  profiles	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom	  and	  for	  
the	   two	   classrooms	   with	   retrofits	   installed.	   Diagrams	   for	   all	   weeks	   and	   classes,	   including	  
temperature	   profiles,	   can	   be	   found	   in	   Appendix	   XI.	   The	   recommended	   limit	   for	   the	   CO2	  
concentration	  of	  1000	  ppm,	  according	  to	  section	  4.1,	  is	  shown	  as	  a	  black	  horizontal	  line	  in	  the	  
diagrams.	  
==PAGE:66==

FIGURE	  6-­‐30:	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  DURING	  THE	  THREE	  WEEKS	  IN	  THE	  CLASSROOM	  WITH	  AUTOMATIC	  WINDOW	  OPENING	  
WITH	  FAN.	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015.	  
Figure	  6-­‐28	  shows	  the	  CO2	  profile	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom	  during	  the	  three	  test	  weeks.	  The	  
maximum	  CO2	  concentration	  was	  around	  2000	  ppm	  more	  or	  less	  all	  days,	  thus	  exceeding	  the	  
recommended	  limit	  of	  1000	  ppm	  according	  to	  BR10.	  Both	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  had	  likewise	  
maximum	   CO2	   levels	   of	   around	   2000	   ppm	   in	   the	   off	   week	   –	   one	   day	   the	   CO2	   concentration	  
reached	  3000	  ppm	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  Figure	  6-­‐29,	  and	  also	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  
FAN	  had	  a	  day	  with	  a	  CO2	  concentration	  above	  2500	  ppm,	  Figure	  6-­‐30.	  For	  the	  classroom	  with	  
AWO,	  the	  effect	  of	  turning	  on	  the	  systems	  appeared	  to	  be	  quite	  small,	  and	  during	  an	  on	  week	  the	  
CO2	   concentration	   still	   reached	   levels	   above	   2500	   ppm.	   Turning	   on	   the	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   system	  
reduced	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  and	  kept	  it	  more	  or	  less	  below	  1500	  ppm.	  
The	  figures	  show	  a	  low	  CO2	  concentration	  on	  Friday	  in	  the	  second	  measuring	  week	  (1st	  of	  May	  
2015),	  which	  is	  due	  to	  this	  Friday	  being	  a	  holiday,	  and	  the	  school	  was	  therefore	  closed.	  
In	  addition	  to	  the	  variation	  in	  CO2	  concentration	  during	  a	  week,	  the	  weekly	  average,	  minimum	  
and	  maximum	  concentrations	  during	  the	  occupied	  hours	  are	  shown	  in	  Table	  6-­‐9.

From	  Table	  6-­‐9	  it	  is	  seen	  that	  the	  average	  CO2	  concentrations	  in	  all	  classrooms	  were	  generally	  
quite	  low	  –	  even	  in	  the	  off	  weeks.	  The	  reference	  classroom	  had	  the	  lowest	  average	  CO2	  level	  in	  
all	  three	  weeks,	  even	  though	  no	  system	  was	  installed;	  in	  all	  weeks	  the	  average	  CO2	  concentration	  
was	   below	   the	   recommended	   level	   of	   1000	   ppm.	   The	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   system	   reduced	   both	   the	  
average	  and	  maximum	  values;	  the	  average	  CO2	  concentration	  got	  below	  1000	  ppm	  in	  both	  on	  
weeks,	  and	  the	  standard	  deviation	  was	  also	  lower,	  indicating	  less	  fluctuations	  in	  the	  on	  weeks.	  
Also	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  had	  a	  lower	  CO2	  concentration	  during	  on	  weeks,	  but	  in	  week	  3	  –	  
being	  an	  on	  week	  –	  the	  maximum	  CO2	  concentration	  still	  reached	  levels	  above	  2700	  ppm.	  
Figure	   6-­‐31	   shows	   the	   cumulated	   percentage	   of	   occupied	   hours	   at	   or	   below	   a	   specific	   CO2	  
concentration.	  The	  acceptable	  (1000	  ppm)	  and	  maximum	  recommended	  (2000	  ppm)	  CO2	  levels	  
are	  shown	  as	  black	  vertical	  lines.

From	  Figure	  6-­‐31,	  it	  can	  be	  seen	  that	  turning	  the	  systems	  on	  brought	  down	  the	  percentage	  of	  
occupied	  hours	  above	  1000	  ppm	  from	  50	  %	  for	  AWO	  and	  60	  %	  for	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  to	  around	  30	  %	  
for	  both	  systems.	  However,	  there	  was	  a	  similar	  decrease	  in	  the	  percentage	  of	  occupied	  hours	  
above	   1000	   ppm	   from	   the	   “off”	   week	   to	   the	   “on”	   weeks	   for	   the	   reference	   classroom	   as	   well,	  
indicating	  that	  the	  decrease	  seen	  for	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  was	  probably	  not	  only	  
due	   to	   the	   effect	   of	   the	   systems,	   but	   perhaps	   also	   affected	   by	   other	   factors,	   such	   as	   outdoor	  
conditions	  or	  user	  behaviour.

observations	   from	   pupils	   having	   completed	   minimum	   one	   test	   in	   each	   of	   the	   two	   conditions

observations	  from	  the	  reference	  classroom.

subtraction	  problems	  –	  and	  those	  were	  excluded	  from	  the	  analysis.	  For	  unknown	  reasons,	  three
