As	  seen	  during	  heating	  season,	  only	  modest	  variation	  in	  PE	  and	  speed	  was	  seen	  for	  all	  tests,	  but	  
the	  relatively	  large	  standard	  deviation	  suggests	  some	  dispersion	  in	  the	  pupils’	  responses	  within	  
each	  class.	  	  
Figure	  6-­‐32	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐33	  show	  the	  weekly	  mean	  PE	  and	  speed	  for	  the	  graphical/grammatical	  
reasoning	  test	  during	  the	  three	  weeks.	  In	  the	  reference	  classroom	  the	  test	  was	  performed	  only	  
in	  the	  two	  first	  weeks;	  PE	  was	  similar	  in	  both	  weeks,	  whereas	  the	  speed	  was	  higher	  in	  week	  2	  
than	  in	  week	  1.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  the	  test	  was	  only	  performed	  in	  week	  2	  and	  3;	  both	  
PE	  and	  speed	  were	  quite	  similar	  in	  those	  weeks.	  In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  the	  pupils	  
made	  fewer	  errors	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  off,	  whereas	  their	  speed	  did	  not	  change	  much	  during	  
the	  three	  weeks.	  
The	  weekly	  mean	  PE	  and	  speed	  for	  the	  math	  test	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐34	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐35.	  No	  
clear	  change	  in	  PE	  was	  seen	  for	  either	  the	  reference	  classroom	  or	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  
FAN,	  whereas	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  made	  slightly	  more	  errors	  in	  week	  3,	  being	  
an	  on	  week.	  Regarding	  speed	  no	  significant	  difference	  was	  seen	  for	  three	  weeks.	  
Figure	  6-­‐36	  shows	  the	  differences	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  for	  standardised	  PE	  and	  
speed	  for	  all	  test	  results	  combined,	  including	  graphical/grammatical	  reasoning	  and	  math	  test.	  	  
The	  P-­‐values	  were	  found	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.9	  with	  Model	  A	  (PE)	  and	  B	  (speed)	  testing	  
for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  
The	  diagram	  was	  made	  so	  that	  a	  negative	  result	  indicates	  that	  the	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  errors	  or	  
worked	  slower	  with	  the	  systems	  on.	  
==PAGE:72==
	  
	  
	  
60	  
	  
FIGURE	  6-­‐36:	  DIFFERENCES	  BETWEEN	  OFF	  AND	  ON	  CONDITIONS	  FOR	  PE	  AND	  SPEED	  FOR	  THE	  TWO	  SYSTEMS	  WITH	  BOTH	  
TESTS	  INCLUDED.	  THE	  VALUES	  ARE	  GIVEN	  AS	  THE	  DIFFERENCE	  IN	  MEAN	  +/-­‐	  S.D.	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015.	  
Figure	  6-­‐36	  shows	  a	  tendency	  towards	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  working	  slightly	  
faster,	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system,	  whereas	  no	  clear	  difference	  between	  on	  and	  off	  condition	  
was	  seen	  for	  PE.	  The	  opposite	  seemed	  to	  be	  the	  case	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  where	  
the	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  errors,	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system,	  while	  almost	  no	  change	  was	  seen	  for	  
speed.	  In	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  PE	  did	  not	  change	  much	  over	  the	  weeks,	  but	  the	  pupils’	  speed	  
decreased	  slightly	  in	  the	  “on”	  weeks.	  Though,	  none	  of	  the	  results	  were	  significant.	  
The	  analysis	  was	  repeated	  separately	  for	  the	  tests	  and	  the	  results	  are	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐37	  and	  
Figure	   6-­‐38.	   The	   P-­‐values	   were	   found	   as	   described	   in	   section	   5.3.9	   with	   Model	   C	   (PE)	   and	   D	  
(speed)	   testing	   for	   the	   effect	   of	   Condition.	   The	   diagrams	   were	   made	   so	   that	   a	   negative	   result	  
indicates	  that	  the	  pupils	  made	  fewer	  errors	  or	  worked	  slower	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  on.

62	  
Figure	   6-­‐37	   shows	   the	   difference	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off	   conditions	   for	   PE	   for	   the	   two	   tests	  
separately,	  suggesting	  no	  systematic	  difference	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  weeks;	  in	  four	  out	  of	  six	  
cases,	  only	  insignificant	  differences	  were	  seen.	  However,	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  
FAN	  made	  significantly	  more	  errors	  in	  the	  graphical/grammatical	  reasoning	  test	  when	  turning	  
on	  the	  system	  (P	  =	  0.01),	  which	  is	  also	  clearly	  seen	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐32,	  and	  also	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  
classroom	  with	  AWO	  made	  noticeably	  more	  errors	  in	  the	  math	  test	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on	  (P	  
=	  0.06).	  This	  is	  the	  exact	  opposite	  tendency	  than	  observed	  in	  the	  heating	  season.	  No	  noteworthy	  
differences	  were	  seen	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  regarding	  PE.	  	  
Regarding	  speed,	  shown	  in	  Figure	  6-­‐38,	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  performed	  
the	  math	  test	  significantly	  slower	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on	  (P	  =	  0.007),	  whereas	  they	  worked	  
slightly	  faster	  in	  the	  graph	  test	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on.	  The	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  
performed	  both	  tests	  slightly	  faster	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system.	  In	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  the	  
pupils	  worked	  significantly	  slower	  when	  making	  the	  graph	  test	  in	  the	  “on”	  weeks	  (P	  =0.002),	  
whereas	  they	  performed	  the	  math	  test	  significantly	  faster	  (P	  =	  0.003).	  
For	  both	  PE	  and	  speed	  there	  was	  no	  systematic	  tendency	  with	  regard	  to	  the	  difference	  between	  
the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions,	  and	  therefore	  it	  is	  likely	  that	  the	  effect	  of	  condition	  depended	  on	  test	  
type.	   Therefore	   the	   interaction	   between	   test	   type	   and	   condition	   (on/off)	   was	   analysed	   with	  
Model	  A	  and	  B,	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  testing	  for	  interaction;	  see	  Figure	  6-­‐39	  to	  Figure	  
6-­‐44.

For	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  no	  interaction	  was	  seen	  between	  the	  effect	  of	  condition	  and	  test	  
type	  on	  PE,	  Figure	  6-­‐39,	  but	  test	  type	  alone	  had	  a	  significant	  effect	  on	  PE	  (P	  =	  0.02).	  Regarding	  
speed,	   Figure	   6-­‐40,	   there	   was	   significant	   interaction	   between	   condition	   and	   test	   type	   (P	   =	  
0.003);	   this	   is	   also	   seen	   from	   the	   two	   graphs	   crossing	   each	   other.	   Furthermore,	   this	   analysis	  
showed	   a	   trend	   towards	   significance	   for	   the	   interaction	   between	   condition	   and	   test	   type	   for	  
AWO	  –	  PE	  (P	  =	  0.05),	  Figure	  6-­‐41,	  and	  a	  significant	  interaction	  for	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  –	  PE	  (P	  =	  0.03),

Figure	  6-­‐43.	  Furthermore,	  some	  interaction	  was	  seen	  between	  condition	  and	  test	  type	  for	  speed	  
in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  (P	  =	  0.06),	  according	  to	  Figure	  6-­‐44.	  Figure	  6-­‐42	  reveals	  no

and	  it	  appeared	  that	  only	  the	  type	  of	  test	  influenced	  the	  speed	  (P	  <	  0.001).	  
As	   for	   the	   heating	   season,	   no	   systematic	   patterns	   were	   found	   in	   the	   interaction,	   and	   the	  
mechanisms,	  which	  caused	  this	  interaction,	  were	  not	  detected.	  
6.2.3. 	  THE	  PUPILS’	  PERCEPTION	  OF	  THE	  INDOOR	  CLIMATE	  AND	  THEIR	  SYMPTOMS	  
In	  this	  section,	  the	  results	  from	  the	  questionnaires,	  completed	  by	  the	  pupils,	  will	  be	  presented.	  
The	  same	  inclusion	  criterion	  used	  for	  the	  observations	  in	  the	  performance	  tests	  was	  also	  used	  
here.	  
Table	  6-­‐13	  shows	  the	  number	  of	  observations	  used	  in	  the	  data	  processing	  for	  the	  three	  classes.

FIGURE	  6-­‐47:	  PERCEPTIONS	  AND	  SYMPTOMS	  FROM	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  COMPLETED	  BY	  THE	  PUPILS	  IN	  THE	  
CLASSROOM	  WITH	  AUTOMATIC	  WINDOW	  OPENING	  WITH	  FAN-­‐ASSISTED	  EXHAUST.	  	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015.	  
In	   the	   reference	   classroom,	   Figure	   6-­‐45,	   no	   significant	   difference	   in	   either	   perceptions	   or	  
symptoms	  was	  seen	  between	  the	  three	  test	  weeks,	  but	  nevertheless	  the	  pupils	  were	  generally	  
more	  unsatisfied	  within	  the	  parameters	  temperature,	  air	  quality,	  concentration;	  their	  discontent	  
with	  the	  temperature	  was	  probably	  due	  to	  the	  classroom	  being	  too	  hot,	  as	  can	  be	  seen	  from	  the	  
diagram.	  	  
The	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  were	  generally	  satisfied	  with	  all	  parameters	  and	  their	  
perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  did	  not	  change	  noticeably	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system,	  according	  to	  
Figure	  6-­‐46.	  
As	  for	  the	  other	  two	  classrooms,	  there	  were	  no	  large	  differences	  in	  perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  
during	   the	   three	   test	   weeks	   in	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN,	   Figure	   6-­‐47.	   Though	   a	   few	  
fluctuations	  were	  seen;	  the	  pupils	  were	  more	  unsatisfied	  with	  the	  air	  quality	  in	  week	  1,	  being	  an	  
on	  week,	  and	  they	  felt	  draught	  in	  week	  2,	  being	  an	  off	  week.	  
To	  be	  able	  to	  examine	  differences	  between	  the	  three	  classes	  regarding	  the	  pupils’	  symptoms	  and	  
perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate,	  the	  same	  diagram	  was	  made	  with	  all	  systems	  and	  the	  reference	  
classroom	  included,	  distinguishing	  between	  on	  and	  off	  condition;	  see	  Figure	  6-­‐48.

FIGURE	  6-­‐48:	  PERCEPTIONS	  AND	  SYMPTOMS	  FROM	  THE	  QUESTIONNAIRES	  FOR	  THE	  DIFFERENT	  SYSTEMS	  TURNED	  ON	  AND	  
OFF	  AND	  FOR	  THE	  REFERENCE	  CLASSROOM.	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  2015.	  
According	  to	  Figure	  6-­‐48,	  there	  was	  no	  effect	  of	  turning	  on	  the	  systems	  in	  the	  two	  classrooms	  
with	  retrofits.	  Furthermore,	  there	  were	  no	  differences	  between	  the	  “on”	  and	  “off”	  conditions	  in	  
the	   reference	   classroom.	   The	   pupils	   generally	   scored	   the	   symptoms	   and	   perceptions	   of	   the	  
indoor	  climate	  in	  the	  same	  range	  regardless	  of	  class,	  but	  with	  some	  exceptions;	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  
classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  were	  more	  sleepy	  and	  felt	  the	  symptoms	  sleepy,	  eyes,	  throat	  and	  
skin	  more	  than	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  two	  other	  classes,	  whereas	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  
AWO	  were	  more	  awake	  and	  found	  it	  easier	  to	  concentrate.	  	  
The	  differences	  in	  perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  are	  shown	  in	  
Figure	  6-­‐49	  and	  Figure	  6-­‐50.	  The	  significance	  levels	  for	  the	  four	  highest	  differences	  are	  shown	  in	  
the	  diagrams;	  those	  were	  good	  week	  in	  school,	  draught,	  temperature	  and	  headache	  for	  AWO	  and	  
hot,	   cold,	   draught	   and	   desire	   to	   work	   for	   AWO	   +	   FAN.	   The	   P-­‐values	   are	   found	   with	   Model	   E,	  
testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition,	  as	  described	  in	  section	  5.3.9.	  
A	  positive	  result	  indicates	  an	  improvement	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  
their	  symptoms.

Figure	  6-­‐49	  shows	  the	  difference	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  
the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  their	  symptoms	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO.	  Generally,	  no	  differences	  
were	  observed,	  except	  for	  a	  significant	  worsening	  of	  headache	  when	  turning	  on	  the	  system	  (P	  =	  
0.03),	  but	  also	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  this	  tendency	  was	  seen	  –	  though	  not	  significant.	  	  
For	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN,	   shown	   in	   Figure	   6-­‐50,	   a	   significant	   improvement	   in	   the	  
perception	  of	  draught	  (P	  =	  0.007)	  was	  seen,	  and	  the	  pupils	  also	  felt	  a	  larger	  desire	  to	  work	  (P	  =	  
0.05)	   with	   the	   system	   turned	   on;	   although	   this	   was	   borderline	   significant.	   These	   tendencies	  
were	  not	  seen	  for	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  so	  it	  might	  be	  a	  result	  of	  the	  system.	  Furthermore,	  
there	   was	   a	   slight	   indication	   of	   the	   pupils	   feeling	   warmer	   when	   turning	   on	   the	   system;	   the	  
change	  in	  the	  perception	  of	  the	  temperature	  in	  the	  classroom	  was	  not	  consistent	  with	  the	  fact	  
that	   the	   temperature	   did	   not	   change	   significantly	   when	   turning	   on	   the	   system,	   according	   to	  
Table	  6-­‐10.	  The	  same	  was	  seen	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  so	  it	  might	  be	  due	  to	  other	  factors,	  
such	  as	  the	  weather	  conditions.	  	  
6.2.1. 	  SUMMARY	  OF	  THE	  P-­‐VALUES	  FOUND	  FROM	  THE	  STATISTICAL	  ANALYSIS	  OF	  THE	  
SYSTEMS’	  EFFECTS	  ON	  THE	  PUPILS’	  PERFORMANCE	  AND	  PERCEPTIONSS,	  NON-­‐HEATING	  
SEASON	  2015	  
In	   the	   following,	   the	   results	   from	   the	   statistical	   analysis	   of	   the	   systems’	   effect	   on	   pupils’	  
performance	   and	   perception	   are	   summarized.	   Also	   the	   applied	   test	   method	   is	   mentioned	   –	  
either	  the	  linear	  mixed	  model	  analysis	  (LMM)	  or	  the	  Wilcoxon	  test.	  
PERFORMANCE	  
Table	   6-­‐14	  summarizes	   the	   results	   from	   the	   analysis	   with	   one	   system	   and	   all	   tests	   included;	  
Model	  A	  and	  B,	  explained	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  were	  used	  to	  analyse	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition	  and	  Test	  
and	  the	  interaction	  between	  the	  two.	  Table	  6-­‐15	  presents	  the	  results	  from	  the	  analysis	  with	  each	  
test	  and	  each	  system	  separately;	  Model	  C	  and	  D,	  presented	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  were	  applied	  to	  test	  
for	   the	   effect	   of	   Condition.	   The	   effects	   marked	   with	   (#)	   are	   those,	   which	   were	   borderline	  
significant,	  meaning	  that	  there	  might	  be	  a	  trend	  towards	  significance.

PERCEPTION	  
Based	   on	   Figure	   6-­‐49	   and	   Figure	   6-­‐50	   –	   showing	   the	   difference	   between	   the	   on	   and	   off

climate	  and	  their	  symptoms	  has	  been	  analysed	  statistically.	  Only	  the	  P-­‐values	  for	  perceptions	  
with	  the	  four	  highest	  differences	  between	  on	  and	  off	  condition	  have	  been	  estimated	  with	  Model	  
E,	  as	  explained	  in	  section	  5.3.9,	  testing	  for	  the	  effect	  of	  Condition.	  The	  results	  are	  summarized	  in	  
Table	  6-­‐16	  below.

6.3. 
AIR	  CHANGE	  RATE	  
The	   air	   change	   rate	   was	   measured	   using	   tracer	   gas,	   as	   described	   in	   subsection	   5.3.3.	   The	  
resulting	   air	   change	   rates	   and	   ventilation	   rates	   are	   shown	   in	   Table	   6-­‐17,	   and	   the	   ventilation	  
rates	   are	   shown	   with	   different	   units	   in	   preparation	   for	   comparing	   with	   different	   laws	   and	  
standards.	  The	  air	  change	  rates	  were	  measured	  over	  two	  days	  for	  practical	  reasons.	  The	  systems	  
marked	  with	  (1)	  were	  measured	  on	  9th	  of	  March	  2015	  and	  those	  marked	  with	  (2)	  on	  8th	  of	  May	  
2015.

–	  and	  more	  or	  less	  within	  the	  same	  range	  for	  all	  three	  classrooms;	  between	  0.30	  h-­‐1	  and	  0.60	  h-­‐1.

rate	  to	  3.29	  h-­‐1,	  and	  the	  highest	  air	  change	  rate	  of	  4.79	  h-­‐1	  was	  found	  in	  the	  reference	  room	  with	  
all	  windows	  open.	  	  
6.4. 
ENERGY	  CONSUMPTION	  	  
A	  system	  that	  may	  promote	  an	  acceptable	  indoor	  climate	  in	  the	  classrooms,	  which	  can	  replace	  
the	  existing	  principle	  with	  only	  manual	  window	  opening,	  may	  also	  use	  more	  energy.	  It	  has	  thus	  
been	  investigated	  how	  much	  energy	  the	  retrofits	  used,	  in	  proportion	  to	  how	  well	  the	  systems	  
provided	  improved	  indoor	  air	  quality.	  Only	  the	  energy	  consumption	  for	  the	  heating	  season	  was	  
included,	   due	   to	   the	   time	   limit	   of	   this	   project.	   The	   energy	   consumption	   was	   monitored	   as	  
described	  in	  section	  5.3.8.	  	  
In	   Table	   6-­‐18	   the	   consumption	   of	   electricity	   (from	   the	   systems)	   and	   heating	   energy	   (from	  
radiators/convectors)	  is	  shown;	  on	  weeks	  are	  shaded.	  The	  energy	  consumption	  is	  shown	  per	  
week	   during	   the	   six-­‐week	   measuring	   period	   in	   the	   heating	   season,	   Table	   6-­‐18.	   For	   practical	  
reasons,	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  in	  which	  the	  energy	  consumption	  for	  heating	  was	  monitored,	  
was	  not	  the	  same	  classroom	  as	  was	  used	  as	  a	  reference	  in	  the	  rest	  of	  the	  project.	  In	  the	  table,	  the	  
R	   stands	   for	   the	   radiator	   placed	   under	   the	   windows	   in	   the	   facade,	   and	   C	   stands	   for	   the	  
convectors	  under	  the	  clerestory	  windows.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:85==

Table	   6-­‐18	   shows	   that	   turning	   on	   the	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   system	   resulted	   in	   higher	   electricity	  
consumption,	  whereas	  no	  difference	  was	  seen	  for	  the	  AWO	  system.	  The	  difference	  between	  the	  
on	  and	  off	  conditions	  for	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  can	  be	  ascribed	  the	  electricity	  use	  of	  the	  fan.	  
Also,	  Table	  6-­‐18	  indicates	  that	  both	  systems	  used	  electricity	  in	  the	  off	  weeks,	  which	  is	  because	  of	  
the	   sensor	   modules	   in	   the	   classrooms	   logging	   CO2	   concentration,	   temperature	   and	   relative	  
humidity	  and	  the	  operating	  switch	  for	  manual	  control	  of	  the	  windows	  still	  used	  electricity	  in	  the	  
off	  weeks.	  
It	   is	   seen	   that	   the	   radiators	   under	   the	   windows	   in	   the	   façade	   used	   0	   kWh	   in	   all	   six	   weeks	   –	  
indicating	  that	  they	  were	  either	  not	  turned	  on	  or	  the	  heating	  consumption	  was	  by	  mistake	  not	  
logged.	  	  
In	  the	  reference	  classroom	  and	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  the	  energy	  consumption	  for	  heating	  
by	  the	  convectors	  increased	  with	  decreasing	  outdoor	  air	  temperature,	  which	  decreased	  from	  an	  
average	  of	  11.6	  °C	  in	  the	  first	  test	  week	  to	  3.4	  °C	  in	  the	  final	  test	  week,	  according	  to	  Table	  6-­‐3.	  	  
In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  more	  heating	  was	  used	  in	  the	  weeks	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  
on.	  For	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  the	  difference	  in	  heating	  consumption	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  
conditions	  seemed	  to	  increase	  with	  decreasing	  outdoor	  temperature.	  In	  the	  first	  two	  test	  weeks,	  
only	  a	  small	  increase	  was	  seen	  from	  the	  off	  to	  the	  on	  week.	  During	  the	  two	  following	  test	  weeks,	  
there	  was	  a	  larger	  difference	  between	  on	  and	  off	  weeks,	  as	  the	  heating	  consumption	  decreased	  
from	  60	  kWh	  in	  week	  3	  (on)	  to	  51	  kWh	  in	  week	  4	  (off).	  The	  largest	  difference	  was	  seen	  from	  
week	  5	  to	  week	  6	  with	  an	  increase	  of	  23	  kWh.	  The	  heating	  consumption	  was	  quite	  similar	  for	  the	  
three	  off	  weeks.	  	  
For	  all	  classrooms,	  the	  energy	  consumption	  for	  heating	  was	  seen	  to	  be	  the	  main	  part	  of	  the	  total	  
energy	  consumption.	  
To	  be	  able	  to	  compare	  the	  two	  systems	  regarding	  efficiency,	  the	  energy	  needed	  for	  the	  systems	  
to	  reduce	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  with	  100	  ppm	  during	  the	  heating	  season	  was	  estimated.	  The	  
data	  for	  the	  calculation	  can	  be	  found	  in	  Appendix	  XIII.	  The	  energy	  trade-­‐off	  for	  the	  AWO	  system	  
to	  lower	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  by	  100	  ppm,	  is	  8.9	  kWh,	  whereas	  for	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  it	  is	  
8.4	  kWh.	  The	  installed	  retrofits	  thus	  needed	  more	  or	  less	  the	  same	  amount	  of	  energy	  to	  reduce	  
the	  CO2	  concentration	  by	  100	  ppm.	  
==PAGE:86==
	  
	  
	  
74	  
This	  is	  a	  simplified	  estimation	  and	  it	  may	  be	  discussed	  whether	  three	  weeks	  are	  sufficient	  to	  
represent	  well	  the	  energy	  use	  of	  the	  systems.	  	  
6.5. 
SUMMARY	  OF	  THE	  RESULTS	  REGARDING	  THE	  SYSTEMS’	  PERFORMANCE	  IN	  
THE	  HEATING	  AND	  THE	  NON-­‐HEATING	  SEASON	  
Table	   6-­‐19	   summarizes	   the	   main	   results	   from	   this	   study,	   given	   as	   mean(s.d.),	   except	   for	   the	  
percentage	  above	  1000	  ppm,	  which	  is	  given	  in	  %.	  	  
The	  indoor	  climate	  parameters	  shown	  in	  the	  table	  are	  CO2	  concentration,	  percentage	  of	  occupied	  
hours	  with	  a	  CO2	  concentration	  above	  1000	  ppm	  and	  temperature,	  shown	  as	  mean	  values	  of	  on	  
(“on”)	  and	  off	  (“off”)	  weeks.	  PE	  [-­‐]	  and	  speed	  [-­‐],	  which	  represent	  the	  pupils’	  performance,	  are	  
shown	  as	  the	  difference	  between	  on	  and	  off	  condition	  –	  (On	  –	  Off)	  or	  (“On”–	  “Off”);	  a	  negative	  
difference	  indicates	  that	  PE	  decreased	  or	  the	  pupils	  worked	  slower	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  on.	  
Furthermore,	   all	   perceptions	   and	   symptoms	   from	   the	   questionnaires	   are	   shown	   as	   the	  
difference	   between	   the	   off	   and	   on	   condition	   –	   (Off	   –	   On)	   or	   (“Off”	   –	   “On”);	   a	   positive	   result	  
indicates	  an	  improvement	  in	  the	  perceptions	  or	  symptoms.	  All	  statistically	  significant	  results	  are	  
indicated	  with	  an	  *.	  The	  missing	  values	  are	  results,	  which	  are	  only	  found	  in	  either	  heating	  or	  
non-­‐heating	  season.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:87==

77	  
7. DISCUSSION	  
In	   the	   three	   classrooms,	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   reached	   levels	   above	   the	   recommended	   1000	  
ppm	   when	   none	   of	   the	   systems	   were	   running.	   During	   the	   heating	   season	   the	   percentage	   of	  
occupied	  hours	  above	  1000	  ppm	  was	  70	  %	  for	  all	  three	  classrooms,	  when	  the	  ventilation	  was	  
handled	  only	  by	  manual	  window	  opening.	  There	  are	  200	  school	  days	  per	  year	  in	  Denmark,	  and	  
approximately	  130	  days	  are	  within	  the	  heating	  season	  (the	  heating	  season	  in	  Denmark	  extends	  
from	  1/10	  to	  1/5).	  70	  %	  corresponds	  to	  approximately	  90	  days,	  where	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  is	  
unacceptable,	   suggesting	   a	   great	   potential	   for	   investing	   in	   alternative	   solutions	   to	   manual	  
window	  opening.	  	  
7.1. 
INDOOR	  ENVIRONMENT	  AND	  VENTILATION	  RATE	  
An	   association	   between	   the	   installed	   ventilation	   systems	   and	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   in	   the	  
classrooms	  was	  observed;	  the	  systems	  were	  able	  to	  lower	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  considerably,	  
which	  was	  expected	  according	  to	  previous	  studies	  (Gao	  et	  al.	  2014).	  The	  effect	  was	  greatest	  in	  
the	  heating	  season,	  and	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  provided	  a	  relatively	  larger	  effect	  on	  the	  CO2	  
concentration	   than	   the	   AWO	   system	   alone.	   Where	   the	   average	   CO2	   concentration	   in	   the	   on	  
condition	  was	  still	  above	  the	  recommended	  level	  of	  1000	  ppm	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  the	  
AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  was	  able	  to	  keep	  the	  average	  concentration	  below	  the	  recommended	  level.	  
However,	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  still	  reached	  levels	  higher	  than	  1000	  ppm,	  which	  could	  mean	  
that	  the	  system	  was	  undersized.	  
In	   the	   non-­‐heating	   season,	   the	   average	   CO2	   concentrations	   were	   lower	   than	   in	   the	   heating	  
season;	  also	  the	  peaks	  in	  the	  off	  condition	  were	  lower.	  The	  effect	  of	  running	  the	  systems	  was	  
generally	   smaller;	   with	   the	   systems	   turned	   on,	   the	   maximum	   CO2	   concentration	   still	   reached	  
levels	  above	  1500	  ppm	  and	  2500	  ppm	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  and	  AWO,	  respectively.	  	  
It	   was	   expected	   that	   the	   effect	   of	   the	   installed	   systems	   on	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   would	   be	  
smaller	  during	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season.	  Previous	  studies	  have	  shown	  that	  the	  windows	  are	  more	  
likely	  to	  be	  opened	  manually	  by	  pupils	  or	  teachers	  during	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season	  (Gao	  et	  al.	  
2014).	   Furthermore,	   the	   average	   outdoor	   air	   temperature	   in	   the	   weeks	   with	   the	   retrofits	  
running	  was	  8.1	  °C	  during	  the	  six	  measuring	  weeks	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  and	  14.2	  °C	  during	  the	  
three	  measuring	  weeks	  in	  non-­‐heating	  season;	  a	  smaller	  difference	  between	  indoor	  and	  outdoor	  
air	   temperature	   causes	   a	   lower	   flow	   and	   thereby	   a	   lower	   effect	   of	   opening	   the	   windows	   –	  
especially	   in	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   with	   no	   exhaust	   fan.	   This	   is	   particularly	   applicable	   for	  
natural	  ventilation,	  for	  which	  the	  effect	  partly	  depends	  on	  the	  outdoor	  conditions,	  such	  as	  wind	  
and	  temperature	  –	  contrary	  to	  e.g.	  a	  mechanical	  ventilation	  system,	  which	  is	  able	  to	  provide	  the	  
required	  indoor	  air	  quality,	  independently	  of	  the	  outdoor	  weather	  conditions.	  
As	   expected,	   only	   modest	   differences	   in	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   between	   the	   weeks	   in	   the	  
reference	  classroom	  were	  observed,	  but	  generally	  the	  peak	  concentrations	  were	  lower	  in	  this	  
classroom	   compared	   to	   the	   two	   other	   classrooms	   in	   the	   off	   condition.	   Perhaps	   because	   the	  
pupils	  or	  teachers	  in	  this	  classroom	  were	  used	  to	  manually	  airing	  out	  by	  opening	  the	  windows	  
themselves.	  In	  the	  two	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  the	  pupils	  and	  teachers	  might	  have	  gotten	  used	  
to	  the	  systems	  taking	  care	  of	  the	  window	  opening,	  so	  during	  off	  weeks	  they	  did	  not	  think	  about	  
==PAGE:90==
	  
	  
	  
78	  
the	   need	   of	   opening	   windows.	   However,	   the	   pupils’	   window	   opening	   behaviour	   has	   not	   been	  
dealt	  with	  here,	  but	  is	  studied	  in	  a	  parallel	  study.	  The	  occupant	  density	  was	  approximately	  the	  
same	  in	  all	  three	  classes	  –	  26	  pupils	  in	  both	  REF	  and	  AWO	  and	  23	  in	  AWO	  +	  FAN.	  
To	  illustrate	  how	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  was	  affected	  by	  the	  retrofits,	  an	  example	  of	  a	  CO2	  profile	  
for	  one	  day	  during	  the	  heating	  season	  is	  shown	  in	  Figure	  7-­‐1;	  also	  the	  temperature	  profile	  is	  
shown.	  	  
	  
FIGURE	  7-­‐1:	  VARIATION	  IN	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  AND	  TEMPERATURE	  DURING	  A	  DAY	  WITH	  AND	  WITHOUT	  THE	  AWO	  +	  
FAN	  SYSTEM	  RUNNING.	  	  
The	  example	  is	  from	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  and	  it	  indicates	  how	  the	  temperature	  and	  
CO2	  concentration	  follow	  each	  other	  during	  a	  day.	  Furthermore,	  this	  figure	  clearly	  demonstrates	  
the	  effect	  of	  running	  the	  system;	  when	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  approaches	  800	  ppm,	  the	  windows	  
open	   and	   after	   a	   short	   while	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   decreases.	   The	   same	   happens	   to	   the	  
temperature,	  but	  with	  a	  small	  delay.	  	  
The	  indoor	  air	  temperature	  in	  the	  classrooms	  was	  not	  directly	  controlled	  by	  the	  system,	  but	  as	  
can	  be	  seen	  from	  Figure	  7-­‐1,	  it	  was	  affected	  by	  the	  operation	  of	  the	  systems.	  As	  a	  side	  effect	  of	  
the	  systems	  opening	  the	  windows,	  the	  average	  temperature	  in	  both	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  
decreased	  about	  1	  °C	  during	  the	  on	  condition	  in	  the	  heating	  season.	  In	  the	  reference	  classroom,	  
the	  same	  tendency	  with	  lower	  temperatures	  in	  the	  “on”	  weeks	  was	  seen	  in	  the	  heating	  season,	  
indicating	  that	  other	  factors	  than	  the	  retrofits	  affected	  the	  temperature,	  as	  for	  example	  outdoor	  
conditions	  (sunny	  weather,	  temperature	  etc.).	  	  
During	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season,	  no	  immediate	  difference	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  in	  
the	  indoor	  air	  temperature	  was	  observed	  in	  the	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits.

CO2	  concentration	  and	  temperature

Temperature	  (off)	  
==PAGE:91==
	  
	  
	  
79	  
BR10	  requires	  a	  ventilation	  rate	  in	  new	  schools	  of	  5	  L/s	  per	  person	  +	  0.35	  L/s	  per	  m2.	  Assuming	  
27	  occupants	  in	  each	  classroom	  (26	  pupils	  and	  one	  teacher)	  and	  knowing	  that	  each	  classroom	  is	  
56	  m2,	  this	  corresponds	  to	  approximately	  155	  L/s.	  Tracer	  gas	  measurements	  showed	  that	  none	  
of	  the	  investigated	  systems	  were	  able	  to	  comply	  with	  this	  requirement,	  but	  with	  a	  ventilation	  
rate	  of	  146	  L/s,	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  was	  close	  to	  comply	  with	  the	  requirement.	  The	  reference	  
classroom	  had	  the	  highest	  ventilation	  rate	  with	  fully	  opened	  windows	  and	  complied	  with	  the	  
requirement	  stated	  in	  BR10,	  even	  though	  no	  system	  was	  installed	  in	  this	  classroom.	  	  
The	  automatic	  window	  opening	  system	  limits	  the	  degree	  of	  opening	  of	  the	  window,	  meaning	  
that	  the	  windows	  in	  the	  reference	  classroom	  had	  a	  higher	  degree	  of	  opening	  in	  the	  fully	  opened	  
condition,	   perhaps	   causing	   the	   higher	   ventilation	   rate	   in	   this	   classroom.	   Furthermore,	   the	  
performance	  of	  the	  systems	  regarding	  ventilation	  rate	  was	  checked	  with	  tracer	  gas	  for	  only	  one	  
weather	  condition	  –	  and	  for	  practical	  reasons	  the	  ventilation	  rate	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  
FAN	  was	  measured	  on	  a	  different	  day	  than	  that	  of	  the	  rest	  of	  the	  classrooms.	  If	  the	  ventilation	  
rates	   were	   measured	   on	   another	   day,	   perhaps	   they	   would	   have	   been	   different	   due	   to	   other	  
weather	   conditions.	   Additionally,	   the	   reference	   classroom	   had	   a	   different	   orientation	   (facing	  
east)	   than	   the	   two	   other	   classrooms	   (facing	   south),	   meaning	   that	   the	   wind	   direction	   has	  
probably	  played	  an	  important	  role	  during	  the	  tracer	  gas	  measurements.	  Nevertheless,	  the	  fact	  
that	  the	  reference	  classroom	  could	  obtain	  the	  highest	  ventilation	  rate	  if	  all	  windows	  were	  fully	  
opened	  might	  be	  a	  contributing	  factor	  in	  the	  lower	  CO2	  concentration	  in	  this	  classroom.	  	  
7.2. 
THE	  PUPILS’	  PERFORMANCE	  
It	  was	  expected	  that	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  would	  improve	  with	  improved	  indoor	  air	  quality,	  
as	  several	  studies	  have	  suggested	  this	  association	  (Haverinen-­‐Shaughnessy	  et	  al.	  2011;	  Petersen	  
et	  al.	  2015;	  Shaughnessy	  et	  al.	  2006;	  Toftum	  et	  al.	  2011;	  Wargocki	  and	  Wyon	  2013).	  
The	  results	  from	  the	  performance	  tests	  completed	  during	  the	  heating	  season	  show	  a	  tendency	  
that	  with	  the	  system	  turned	  on,	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  had	  a	  lower	  error	  
rate,	  and	  they	  worked	  at	  the	  same	  speed,	  whereas	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  worked	  
slower,	  but	  they	  did	  not	  have	  a	  significantly	  lower	  error	  rate.	  Perhaps	  these	  results	  suggest	  that	  
the	   AWO	   +	   FAN	   system	   provided	   good	   enough	   indoor	   air	   quality	   for	   the	   pupils	   to	   be	   more	  
accurate	   in	   their	   work,	   without	   having	   to	   spend	   significantly	   more	   time,	   whereas	   the	   AWO	  
system	  provides	  slightly	  better	  air	  quality	  –	  good	  enough	  for	  the	  pupils	  to	  concentrate	  more,	  
possibly	   resulting	   in	   lower	   speed	   –	   but	   yet	   not	   good	   enough	   for	   them	   to	   have	   a	   significantly	  
lower	  error	  rate.	  	  
The	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  had	  a	  significantly	  higher	  error	  rate	  in	  the	  d2-­‐test	  (P	  <	  
0.001),	  when	  the	  system	  was	  turned	  on,	  which	  was	  unexpected.	  An	  earlier	  study,	  using	  the	  d2-­‐
test	  to	  investigate	  pupils’	  performance,	  showed	  that	  the	  error	  rate	  increased	  with	  increasing	  CO2	  
concentration	  (Twardella	  et	  al.	  2012),	  thus	  suggesting	  that	  a	  lower	  CO2	  concentration	  will	  result	  
in	  a	  lower	  error	  rate.	  The	  weekly	  variation	  in	  error	  rate	  for	  the	  d2-­‐test,	  Figure	  6-­‐11,	  indicates	  
that	  especially	  the	  high	  error	  rate	  in	  week	  6	  may	  have	  affected	  the	  result.	  The	  reason	  for	  this	  is	  
unknown,	   but	   perhaps	   the	   pupils	   were	   bored	   with	   the	   test	   after	   having	   completed	   it	   for	   five	  
weeks,	  and	  therefore	  did	  not	  make	  an	  effort	  to	  complete	  the	  test	  properly	  during	  the	  final	  test	  
week.	  	  
==PAGE:92==
	  
	  
	  
80	  
As	  the	  error	  rate	  decreased	  when	  the	  systems	  were	  on,	  the	  hypothesis	  for	  this	  study	  to	  some	  
degree	  may	  be	  accepted.	  Contrary	  to	  expectation	  the	  pupils	  performed	  the	  tests	  slower	  in	  the	  on	  
condition	  in	  the	  heating	  season,	  which	  was	  not	  consistent	  with	  the	  hypothesis,	  suggesting	  that	  
the	   speed	   increased	   when	   the	   systems	   were	   running.	   Furthermore,	   it	   was	   contrary	   to	  
expectation	  compared	  to	  previous	  studies,	  claiming	  a	  higher	  speed	  at	  higher	  ventilation	  rates	  
(Wargocki	  and	  Wyon	  2013).	  The	  same	  study	  has	  suggested	  that	  the	  performance	  in	  terms	  of	  
speed	  was	  improved	  for	  every	  1	  °C	  the	  temperature	  was	  reduced	  below	  25	  °C;	  in	  this	  study,	  the	  
temperature	   actually	   did	   decrease,	   but	   it	   did	   not	   cause	   a	   higher	   speed.	   Perhaps	   the	   pupils	  
worked	  more	  concentrated	  in	  the	  on	  weeks	  due	  to	  the	  improved	  air	  quality,	  causing	  the	  lower	  
speed;	  maybe	  they	  were	  told	  by	  the	  teacher	  to	  be	  as	  accurate	  as	  possible.	  In	  both	  seasons	  and	  for	  
both	  systems,	  the	  maximum	  temperatures	  in	  the	  classrooms	  exceeded	  the	  recommended	  range	  
of	   20	   °C	   to	   22	   °C,	   according	   to	   the	   Danish	   Working	   Environment	   Authority.	   Previous	   studies	  
have	   suggested	   that	   the	   performance	   is	   improved	   with	   reduced	   temperatures	   below	   25°C	  
(Wargocki	  and	  Wyon	  2013),	  so	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  temperature	  in	  the	  classrooms	  was	  sometimes	  
on	  the	  warmer	  side	  of	  the	  recommendation	  may	  have	  influenced	  the	  pupils’	  performance.	  
The	  tendency	  seen	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  was	  not	  seen	  during	  non-­‐heating	  season,	  Figure	  6-­‐37	  
and	  Figure	  6-­‐38.	  Perhaps	  this	  was	  due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  only	  three	  weeks	  were	  included	  in	  the	  
non-­‐heating	  season,	  compared	  to	  six	  weeks	  in	  the	  heating	  season.	  Hence,	  the	  results	  from	  the	  
two	  seasons	  were	  based	  on	  a	  different	  number	  of	  observations.	  In	  continuation	  of	  this,	  perhaps	  
the	  systems	  should	  have	  been	  running	  for	  longer	  periods	  of	  time,	  in	  order	  to	  establish	  clear	  and	  
systematic	  tendencies.	  
Finally,	   even	   though	   an	   effect	   of	   the	   systems	   was	   seen	   on	   CO2	   and	   temperature,	   there	   are	   a	  
number	  of	  reasons	  that	  could	  explain	  the	  apparent	  lack	  of	  any	  consistent	  effect	  of	  the	  systems	  on	  
the	  pupils’	  performance	  and	  academic	  achievement.	  This	  study	  has	  been	  a	  demonstration	  study,	  
where	  the	  two	  retrofits	  were	  installed	  to	  see	  how	  they	  worked	  in	  practice,	  without	  controlling	  
and	  taking	  into	  account	  other	  influencing	  factors.	  Furthermore,	  it	  was	  not	  checked,	  which	  time	  
of	  the	  day	  the	  tests	  were	  completed,	  or	  when	  the	  pupils	  had	  lectures	  before	  taking	  the	  tests	  –	  
perhaps	  they	  had	  been	  in	  a	  classroom	  with	  poorer	  or	  better	  air	  quality	  or	  outside.	  	  
Another	  factor,	  which	  might	  have	  influenced	  the	  results,	  is	  lack	  of	  motivation	  from	  the	  pupils;	  
this	  was	  expressed	  by	  comments	  from	  the	  pupils	  on	  the	  performed	  tests	  saying	  that	  the	  tests	  
were	  stupid,	  and	  that	  he	  or	  she	  did	  not	  want	  to	  perform	  the	  test	  –	  some	  even	  made	  drawings	  on	  
the	  tests.	  Also,	  notes	  from	  the	  teachers	  revealed	  that	  some	  pupils	  did	  not	  want	  to	  complete	  the	  
test,	   and	   therefore	   they	   were	   excluded.	   Furthermore,	   these	   tests	   were	   in	   addition	   to	   their	  
ordinary	  schoolwork,	  and	  might	  have	  been	  completed	  close	  to	  e.g.	  breaks	  or	  end	  of	  the	  school	  
day,	  perhaps	  leading	  to	  unfocused	  pupils	  and	  teachers.	  	  
7.3. 
THE	  PUPILS’	  PERCEPTIONS	  OF	  THE	  INDOOR	  ENVIRONMENT	  AND	  THEIR	  
SYMPTOMS	  
The	  pupils’	  subjective	  experience	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  their	  acute	  health	  symptoms	  did	  not	  
vary	  much	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  for	  either	  of	  the	  seasons,	  though	  some	  associations	  
were	  found	  between	  the	  pupils’	  perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  and	  the	  operation	  of	  the	  systems.	  	  
==PAGE:93==
	  
	  
	  
81	  
Unexpectedly,	   during	   the	   heating	   season	   a	   significantly	   higher	   intensity	   of	   the	   stuffy	   nose	  
symptom	   was	   seen	   in	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO,	   Figure	   6-­‐26,	   when	   the	   system	   was	   on	   (P	   =	  
0.002).	   From	   Figure	   6-­‐23	   it	   is	   seen	   that	   it	   is	   only	   one	   week	   (week	   6),	   which	   caused	   the	  
worsening	  in	  this	  symptom,	  so	  perhaps	  the	  pupils	  felt	  a	  stuffier	  nose	  due	  the	  colder	  weather	  
seen	  in	  week	  6	  (3.4	  °C),	  or	  maybe	  this	  result	  was	  simply	  due	  to	  chance.	  
In	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  the	  pupils	  were	  actually	  able	  to	  perceive	  the	  improved	  air	  
quality	  in	  the	  heating	  season,	  as	  there	  was	  a	  significant	  improvement	  in	  the	  perception	  of	  the	  
indoor	   air	   quality	   (P	   <	   0.001).	   Furthermore,	   an	   improvement	   in	   the	   perception	   of	   the	  
temperature	   was	   seen,	   so	   perhaps	   the	   pupils	   did	   feel	   a	   more	   comfortable	   temperature,	  
corresponding	  to	  the	  lowering	  in	  temperature	  seen	  from	  the	  logged	  results,	  though	  the	  change	  
in	   perception	   of	   the	   temperature	   was	   not	   significant.	   When	   talking	   to	   the	   teachers,	   they	  
expressed	  their	  satisfaction	  with	  the	  indoor	  climate;	  they	  also	  perceived	  the	  improved	  indoor	  air	  
quality	  provided	  by	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system.	  
During	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season,	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  had	  more	  headache,	  when	  
the	  system	  was	  turned	  on;	  unexpectedly,	  a	  significant	  increase	  in	  the	  intensity	  of	  the	  headache	  
symptom	  was	  seen	  (P	  =	  0.03).	  Nevertheless,	  Figure	  6-­‐46	  indicates	  that	  the	  pupils’	  score	  of	  the	  
headache	  symptom	  was	  in	  the	  lower	  end	  of	  the	  scale	  in	  both	  on	  and	  off	  weeks,	  suggesting	  that	  
the	  intensity	  of	  this	  symptom	  was	  low	  in	  either	  case,	  so	  perhaps	  the	  slightly	  higher	  intensity	  
with	  the	  system	  turned	  on	  was	  due	  to	  chance.	  
In	   the	   classroom	   with	   AWO	   +	   FAN,	   the	   pupils	   perceived	   significantly	   less	   draught	   during	   the	  
non-­‐heating	  season	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  on	  (P	  =	  0.007)	  –	  perhaps	  due	  to	  a	  higher	  outdoor	  
air	  temperature	  (4	  °C	  higher	  in	  both	  on	  weeks),	  causing	  the	  pupils	  to	  feel	  less	  cold,	  when	  the	  
windows	  were	  open.	  They	  also	  felt	  a	  larger	  desire	  to	  work;	  with	  a	  significance	  level	  of	  P	  =	  0.05,	  
this	  was	  close	  to	  being	  significant	  (borderline	  significant).	  However,	  these	  improvements	  in	  the	  
pupils’	   perceptions	   were	   not	   comparable	   to	   the	   pupils’	   performance,	   which	   showed	   no	  
systematic	  tendencies	  towards	  improvement.	  	  
Overall,	  the	  small	  differences	  between	  the	  on	  and	  off	  conditions	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  
indoor	  climate	  and	  their	  symptoms	  might	  suggest	  that	  the	  pupils	  find	  it	  difficult	  to	  make	  up	  their	  
mind	  about	  how	  they	  perceive	  the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  how	  they	  feel	  about	  e.g.	  specific	  symptoms	  
like	  skin,	  throat	  and	  eyes.	  Perhaps	  the	  scales	  were	  too	  difficult	  for	  the	  pupils	  to	  understand	  –	  
even	  though	  the	  scale	  was	  based	  on	  smileys.	  Furthermore,	  many	  other	  parameters	  may	  have	  
affected	  their	  health	  and	  wellbeing	  in	  the	  test	  weeks,	  such	  as	  disease	  flourishing	  in	  the	  classes,	  
or	  perhaps	  the	  systems	  must	  be	  turned	  on	  for	  longer	  periods	  of	  time	  to	  be	  able	  to	  see	  an	  effect	  
on	  the	  pupils’	  health.	  Additionally,	  it	  is	  unknown	  how	  long	  time	  the	  pupils	  had	  been	  staying	  in	  
the	  classroom	  before	  completing	  the	  questionnaires	  –	  perhaps	  the	  pupils	  should	  have	  stayed	  in	  
the	  classroom	  for	  longer	  time,	  before	  it	  was	  possible	  to	  see	  any	  effect	  on	  their	  perceptions	  and	  
health.	  Furthermore,	  earlier	  studies	  have	  suggested	  that	  at	  CO2	  concentrations	  below	  800	  ppm,	  
BRS	  decreased	  significantly	  (Seppänen	  et	  al.	  1999).	  Perhaps	  the	  decrease	  in	  CO2	  concentration	  
seen	  from	  these	  results	  was	  simply	  not	  sufficient	  to	  affect	  the	  pupils’	  health.	  Finally,	  it	  may	  be	  
the	  case	  that	  the	  pupils	  adapted	  to	  the	  poor	  indoor	  air	  quality.	  These	  factors	  might	  as	  well	  have	  
influenced	  the	  pupils’	  performance.	  	  
==PAGE:94==
	  
	  
	  
82	  
7.4. 
LIMITATIONS	  OF	  RESULTS	  AND	  THE	  PROJECT	  IN	  GENERAL	  
Contrary	  to	  other	  studies,	  this	  was	  a	  demonstration	  study.	  Retrofits	  were	  installed	  in	  classrooms	  
and	  it	  was	  investigated	  how	  they	  worked	  in	  practice,	  without	  controlling	  and	  taking	  into	  account	  
other	   factors,	   which	   might	   have	   changed	   as	   a	   consequence	   of	   installing	   the	   retrofits.	   It	   is	  
therefore	  likely	  that	  other	  factors	  may	  have	  influenced	  the	  results.	  
There	  was	  no	  placebo,	  so	  the	  pupils	  could	  see	  when	  the	  retrofits	  were	  in	  service	  or	  turned	  off.	  
Ideally,	  the	  pupils	  should	  have	  been	  kept	  blind	  to	  the	  interventions	  to	  assure	  that	  they	  were	  not	  
indirectly	  affected	  by	  the	  fact	  that	  they	  knew,	  whether	  the	  retrofits	  were	  running	  or	  not;	  though,	  
with	  visible	  interventions	  like	  opening	  of	  windows,	  this	  would	  probably	  be	  hard	  to	  obtain.	  
It	  was	  not	  checked	  how	  long	  the	  pupils	  had	  been	  in	  the	  classroom	  before	  completing	  either	  the	  
performance	  tests	  or	  the	  questionnaires,	  so	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  pupils	  might	  have	  had	  lectures	  in	  
other	   classrooms	   with	   poorer	   or	   better	   indoor	   climate	   right	   before	   completion	   of	   the	   tasks,	  
might	  have	  influenced	  the	  results.	  	  
A	  basic	  thing	  in	  this	  study	  was	  that	  it	  was	  dealing	  with	  children.	  They	  can	  easily	  get	  distracted	  by	  
their	  surroundings	  –	  for	  example	  by	  noise	  from	  other	  pupils	  or	  something	  interesting	  outside	  
the	   windows.	   Additionally,	   they	   might	   not	   know	   the	   indoor	   climate	   terms	   used	   in	   the	  
questionnaires,	  and	  individual	  differences	  may	  affect	  how	  the	  pupils	  perceive	  the	  indoor	  climate	  
and	  their	  wellbeing.	  
Based	  on	  the	  results	  from	  this	  study,	  it	  is	  difficult	  to	  make	  any	  final	  recommendations	  regarding	  
choice	  of	  ventilation	  system	  in	  classrooms.	  No	  systematic	  tendencies	  on	  the	  effect	  of	  the	  retrofits	  
on	   pupil	   performance,	   their	   perceptions	   or	   their	   health	   were	   found.	   And	   even	   though	   both	  
retrofits	   were	   able	   to	   reduce	   the	   CO2	   concentration	   in	   the	   classrooms,	   it	   exceeded	   the	  
recommended	   1000	   ppm,	   even	   with	   the	   systems	   running.	   Additional	   investigations	   may	   be	  
needed	   to	   make	   conclusive	   recommendations	   regarding	   the	   choice	   of	   ventilation	   system	   in	  
classrooms.	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:95==
	  
	  
	  
83	  
8. IDEAS	  FOR	  FUTURE	  STUDIES	  
Due	  to	  the	  time	  limit	  of	  this	  project,	  it	  was	  not	  possible	  to	  realize	  all	  ideas,	  which	  came	  up	  during	  
the	  project	  period.	  If	  more	  time	  had	  been	  available	  –	  or	  as	  ideas	  for	  future	  studies	  –	  it	  would	  
have	  been	  exciting	  to	  	  
• 
Study	  the	  influence	  on	  the	  indoor	  climate	  and	  the	  pupils	  of	  noise	  from	  the	  systems	  (the	  
fan).	   The	   noise	   level	   will	   be	   a	   contributing	   factor	   in	   evaluating	   the	   performance	   of	   a	  
ventilation	  system,	  and	  too	  high	  noise	  from	  the	  systems	  may	  disturb	  the	  teaching.	  
• 
Study	  window	  and	  door	  opening	  behaviour.	  This	  might	  explain	  some	  of	  the	  tendencies	  
seen	  for	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  and	  the	  temperature;	  another	  study	  has	  studied	  this.	  
• 
Have	   three	   successive	   weeks	   with	   the	   systems	   turned	   on	   and	   three	   successive	   weeks	  
with	  the	  systems	  turned	  off,	  to	  see	  if	  the	  pupils	  learn	  faster	  with	  the	  systems	  turned	  on.	  
• 
Have	  an	  equal	  amount	  of	  weeks	  in	  the	  heating	  and	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season.	  
• 
Make	  a	  cost-­‐benefit	  analysis	  to	  determine	  when	  it	  is	  economically	  beneficial	  to	  invest	  in	  
a	  system	  like	  for	  example	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system,	  and	  when	  it	  is	  a	  better	  solution	  to	  find	  
alternatives	  like	  
o Changing	  the	  daily	  routines,	  for	  example	  as	  a	  topic	  on	  a	  project	  day:	  
! 
Classrooms	  should	  be	  empty	  during	  breaks	  
! 
Venting	  after	  every	  lecture	  as	  a	  minimum	  
! 
Outdoor	  clothes	  should	  be	  kept	  outside	  the	  classroom	  
• 
Examine	  if	  the	  systems	  should	  have	  been	  running	  for	  longer	  periods	  of	  time,	  in	  order	  to	  
establish	  more	  clear	  and	  systematic	  tendencies.	  
• 
Examine	  if	  the	  effect	  was	  postponed,	  so	  that	  the	  effect	  of	  the	  systems	  was	  first	  seen	  the	  
weeks	  after.	  
• 
Find	  out	  what	  the	  pupils	  were	  told	  before	  completing	  the	  performance	  tests	  –	  perhaps	  
the	  pupils	  were	  told	  to	  make	  the	  tests	  as	  accurate	  as	  possible.	  It	  would	  have	  been	  ideal	  to	  
have	  an	  instruction	  for	  the	  teachers	  to	  read	  before	  completion	  of	  the	  tests.	  	  
• 
See	  if	  a	  poor	  indoor	  climate	  affects	  the	  weaker	  pupils	  more.	  Very	  sensitive	  pupils,	  like	  
pupils	  with	  learning	  and	  concentration	  difficulties,	  might	  feel	  the	  negative	  effects	  of	  poor	  
indoor	   climate	   even	   more	   than	   other	   pupils,	   contributing	   to	   an	   increase	   in	   the	   social	  
health	  disparity.	  	  
• 
See	  if	  weekday	  and	  time	  of	  day	  for	  completion	  of	  performance	  tests	  and	  questionnaires	  
influence	  the	  outcome	  of	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  and	  perceptions.	  
• 
See	  if	  the	  effect	  of	  the	  retrofits	  on	  the	  pupils’	  performance	  was	  different	  depending	  on	  
the	   period	   looked	   at.	   By	   averaging	   all	   on	   and	   off	   weeks	   with	   regard	   to	   the	   pupils’	  
performance,	  some	  tendencies	  might	  have	  been	  lost.	  Perhaps	  the	  effect	  of	  the	  systems	  
was	  higher	  in	  the	  first	  weeks	  than	  in	  the	  final	  weeks	  –	  or	  vice	  versa.	  	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:96==
	  
	  
	  
84	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:97==
	  
	  
	  
85	  
9. CONCLUSION	  
By	  investigating	  CO2-­‐based	  control	  of	  automatic	  window	  opening	  with	  and	  without	  fan-­‐assisting	  
exhaust	  as	  an	  alternative	  to	  the	  manual	  window	  opening	  in	  a	  Danish	  elementary	  school,	  some	  
associations	  between	  the	  improved	  indoor	  air	  quality	  provided	  by	  the	  retrofits	  and	  the	  pupils’	  
performance	  and	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  were	  found.	  
Without	  the	  retrofits,	  the	  CO2	  concentration	  in	  the	  classrooms	  reached	  levels	  above	  3000	  ppm,	  
which	   is	   far	   above	   the	   recommended	   level	   according	   to	   BR10	   and	   the	   Danish	   Working	  
Environment	  Authority.	  	  
Analysis	  of	  the	  indoor	  air	  quality	  in	  the	  classrooms	  with	  retrofits	  showed	  that	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  
system	  provided	  the	  best	  indoor	  air	  quality	  by	  lowering	  the	  average	  CO2	  concentration	  to	  an	  
acceptable	  level	  below	  1000	  ppm.	  The	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  system	  was	  able	  to	  reduce	  the	  percentage	  of	  
occupied	  hours	  with	  a	  CO2	  concentration	  above	  1000	  ppm	  from	  70	  %	  to	  20	  %	  in	  the	  heating	  
season	   and	   from	   60	   %	   to	   30	   %	   in	   the	   non-­‐heating	   season.	   The	   AWO	   system	   reduced	   the	  
percentage	  of	  occupied	  hours	  above	  1000	  ppm	  from	  70	  %	  to	  55	  %	  in	  the	  heating	  season	  and	  
from	  50	  %	  to	  30	  %	  in	  the	  non-­‐heating	  season.	  As	  a	  side	  effect,	  both	  systems	  slightly	  decreased	  
the	  air	  temperature	  in	  the	  classrooms,	  and	  in	  both	  cases	  the	  temperature	  was	  kept	  below	  the	  
maximum	   recommended	   level	   of	   25	   °C,	   according	   to	   the	   Danish	   Working	   Environment	  
Authority.	  
Analysis	  of	  pupils’	  performance	  showed	  a	  trend	  that	  the	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  +	  
FAN	   had	   significantly	   lower	   error	   rate	   in	   two	   out	   of	   four	   tests	   with	   the	   retrofits	   running,	  
whereas	  their	  speed	  was	  not	  significantly	  changed.	  The	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO,	  on	  
the	  contrary,	  worked	  significantly	  slower	  in	  three	  out	  of	  four	  tests,	  whereas	  they	  did	  not	  have	  a	  
significantly	   lower	   error	   rate.	   Similar	   tendencies	   were	   not	   found	   in	   the	   non-­‐heating	   season,	  
where	  the	  effect	  of	  the	  systems	  resulted	  in	  both	  lower	  and	  higher	  error	  rate	  and	  slower	  and	  
faster	  speed	  depending	  on	  test	  type.	  
Additionally,	  there	  were	  only	  modest	  differences	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  indoor	  climate	  
and	  their	  symptoms,	  when	  running	  the	  systems.	  However,	  one	  thing	  worth	  mentioning	  is	  that	  
there	  was	  a	  significant	  improvement	  in	  the	  pupils’	  perception	  of	  the	  air	  quality	  in	  the	  classroom	  
with	  AWO	  +	  FAN,	  so	  in	  this	  classroom	  the	  improvement	  of	  the	  air	  quality	  was	  actually	  perceived	  
by	  the	  pupils.	  The	  pupils	  in	  the	  classroom	  with	  AWO	  might	  have	  found	  it	  difficult	  to	  note	  the	  
difference	  between	  the	  conditions	  –	  perhaps	  due	  to	  the	  differences	  in	  the	  indoor	  environment	  
being	  too	  small	  for	  them	  to	  perceive.	  
All	   in	   all,	   there	   were	   no	   systematic	   tendencies	   in	   the	   pupils’	   performance,	   perceptions	   or	  
symptoms,	   so	   probably	   more	   studies	   are	   needed	   to	   clearly	   detect	   effects	   of	   the	   retrofits	   on	  
pupils’	  performance	  and	  perceptions.	  Nevertheless,	  there	  was	  a	  positive	  effect	  of	  the	  AWO	  +	  FAN	  
system	   on	   the	   indoor	   air	   quality	   in	   the	   classroom,	   and	   the	   pupils	   were	   able	   to	   perceive	   this	  
effect;	   perhaps	   some	   improvement	   in	   the	   regulation	   of	   the	   system,	   assuring	   that	   the	   CO2	  
concentration	   stays	   below	   1000	   ppm,	   will	   make	   it	   an	   even	   better	   alternative	   to	   the	   manual	  
airing.	  
==PAGE:98==
	  
	  
	  
86	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:99==

XIII	  
The	   graphical	   user	   interface	   to	   R,	   called	   R	   commander,	   has	   been	   used	   to	   make	   the	   statistical	  
analysis	  in	  R.	  	  
The	  linear	  mixed	  model	  analysis	  requires	  the	  R	  package	  lme4,	  which	  is	  loaded	  by	  
install.packages(“lme4”) 
library(lme4) 
	  
EXAMPLE	  OF	  MODEL	  A	  AND	  B	  
#Loading data 
gram_4a <-  
  read.table("/Users/pigeon/Dropbox/Line/DTU - Kandidat/Speciale, 
F15/Tests and measurements/Tests_2015/Baddeley test/Graph_Class 
4a_Line.csv", 
   header=TRUE, sep=";", na.strings="NA", dec=".", strip.white=TRUE) 
math_4a <-  
  read.table("/Users/pigeon/Dropbox/Line/DTU - Kandidat/Speciale, 
F15/Tests and measurements/Tests_2015/Math test/Math_Class 
4a_Line.csv", 
   header=TRUE, sep=";", na.strings="NA", dec=".", strip.white=TRUE) 
 
#Merge data 
merged_4a <- mergeRows(gram_4a, math_4a, common.only=FALSE) 
 
 
#Creating PE model 
pe.model = lmer(PEnorm ~ Condition + Test + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
summary(pe.model) 
 
 
#Cheking assumptions 
# Linearity + homoskedasticity: 
plot(fitted(pe.model), residuals(pe.model)) 
 
#Normality of residuals: 
qqnorm(residuals(pe.model)) 
 
#Finding p-values, condition ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
pe.null = lmer(PEnorm ~ Test + (1|Initials), data=merged_4a) 
anova(pe.null, pe.model) 
 
#Finding p-values, test ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
pe.null = lmer(PEnorm ~ Condition + (1|Initials), data=merged_4a) 
==PAGE:124==
	  
	  
	  
XIV	  
anova(pe.null, pe.model) 
 
#Testing for interaction 
pe.model = lmer(PEnorm ~ Condition*Test + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
summary(pe.model) 
 
pe.null = lmer(PEnorm ~ Test + Condition + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
anova(pe.null, pe.model) 
 
 
#Creating speed model 
speed.model = lmer(Speednorm ~ Condition + Test + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
summary(speed.model) 
 
 
#Cheking assumptions 
# Linearity + homoskedasticity: 
plot(fitted(speed.model), residuals(speed.model)) 
 
#Normality of residuals: 
qqnorm(residuals(speed.model)) 
 
#Finding p-values, condition ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
speed.null = lmer(Speednorm ~  Test + (1|Initials), data=merged_4a) 
anova(speed.null, speed.model) 
 
#Finding p-values, test ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
speed.null = lmer(Speednorm ~ Condition + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
anova(speed.null, speed.model) 
 
#Testing for interaction 
speed.model = lmer(Speednorm ~ Condition*Test + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
summary(speed.model) 
 
speed.null = lmer(Speednorm ~ Condition + Test + (1|Initials), 
data=merged_4a) 
anova(speed.null, speed.model)	  
	  
==PAGE:125==
	  
	  
	  
XV	  
EXAMPLE	  OF	  MODEL	  C	  AND	  D	  
#Loading data 
gram_4a <-  
  read.table("/Users/pigeon/Dropbox/Line/DTU 
- 
Kandidat/Speciale, 
F15/Tests 
and 
measurements/Tests_2015/Baddeley 
test/Graph_Class 
4a_Line.csv", 
   header=TRUE, sep=";", na.strings="NA", dec=".", strip.white=TRUE) 
 
 
#Creating PE model 
pe.model = lmer(PEnorm ~ Condition + (1|Initials), data=gram_4a) 
summary(pe.model) 
 
 
#Cheking assumptions 
# Linearity + homoskedasticity: 
plot(fitted(pe.model), residuals(pe.model)) 
 
#Normality of residuals: 
qqnorm(residuals(pe.model)) 
 
#Finding p-values ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
pe.null = lmer(PEnorm ~ (1|Initials), data=gram_4a)

#Creating speed model 
speed.model 
= 
lmer(Speednorm 
~ 
Condition 
+ 
(1|Initials), 
data=gram_4a)

#Cheking assumptions 
# Linearity + homoskedasticity: 
plot(fitted(speed.model), residuals(speed.model)) 
 
#Normality of residuals: 
qqnorm(residuals(speed.model)) 
 
#Finding p-values ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
speed.null = lmer(Speednorm ~ (1|Initials), data=gram_4a)

EXAMPLE	  OF	  MODEL	  E	  
#Loading data 
quest_4a <-  
  read.table("/Users/pigeon/Dropbox/Line/DTU - Kandidat/Speciale, 
F15/Tests and measurements/Tests_2014/Questionnaires_Line/Class 
4.a_Line_rådata.csv", 
   header=TRUE, sep=";", na.strings="NA", dec=".", strip.white=TRUE) 
 
 
#Creating cold model 
cold.model = lmer(Cold ~ Condition + (1|Initials), data=quest_4a) 
summary(cold.model) 
 
 
#Cheking assumptions 
# Linearity + homoskedasticity: 
plot(fitted(cold.model), residuals(cold.model)) 
 
#Normality of residuals: 
qqnorm(residuals(cold.model)) 
 
#Finding p-values, condition ("Likelihood Ratio Test") 
cold.null = lmer(Cold ~ (1|Initials), data=quest_4a) 
anova(cold.null, cold.model) 
	  
EXAMPLE	  OF	  WILCOXON	  TEST	  
draught_4a <-  
  read.table("/Users/pigeon/Dropbox/Line/DTU - Kandidat/Speciale, 
F15/Tests and 
measurements/Tests_2015/Questionnaires/Wilcoxon_4a_draught.csv", 
   header=TRUE, sep=";", na.strings="NA", dec=".", strip.white=TRUE) 
 
 
with(draught_4a, mean(Off - On, na.rm=TRUE)) # mean difference 
with(draught_4a, 
wilcox.test(On, 
Off, 
alternative='two.sided', 
paired = TRUE))

XXXVII	  
Table	  I,	  Table	  II	  and	  Table	  III	  show	  the	  average	  score	  and	  the	  standard	  deviation	  for	  the	  different	  
perceptions	  and	  symptoms	  from	  the	  questionnaires.	  The	  results	  have	  been	  divided	  into	  three	  
categories;	  perceptions	  (Table	  I),	  specific	  symptoms	  (Table	  II)	  and	  general	  symptoms	  (Table	  III).	  	  
TABLE	  I:	  PERCEPTIONS.	  VALUES	  ARE	  GIVEN	  AS	  MEAN	  (S.D.).	  HEATING	  SEASON	  2014.	    
Test	  week	  
(Condition)	  
Cold	  
Air	  quality	  
Draught

LIII  
APPENDIX	  XIII	  –	  ENERGY	  CALCULATION	  
==PAGE:164==
	  
	  
	  
LIV  
	  
	  
==PAGE:165==
	  
	  
	  
LV	  
Table	   VIII	   shows	   the	   data	   used	   to	   estimate	   the	   amount	   of	   energy	   needed	   to	   reduce	   the	   CO2	  
concentration	  with	  100	  ppm.	  	  
The	  energy	  consumption	  was	  taken	  as	  an	  average	  over	  each	  of	  the	  three	  test	  weeks	  with	  the	  
systems	  turned	  on	  and	  off.	  Furthermore,	  the	  average	  CO2	  concentration	  in	  on	  and	  off	  condition	  in	  
the	   heating	   season	   during	   the	   occupied	   hours	   was	   used	   for	   this	   purpose.	   The	   energy	  
consumption	  for	  electricity	  was	  multiplied	  with	  energy	  factor	  2.5	  in	  order	  to	  sum	  up	  the	  energy	  
use	  for	  electricity	  and	  heating	  (http://bygbygg.org/energirammer).	  
TABLE	  VIII:	  ENERGY	  USE	  [KWH]	  USED	  TO	  FIND	  THE	  AMOUNT	  OF	  ENERGY	  NEEDED	  TO	  REDUCE	  THE	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  
WITH	  100	  PPM.	  ALSO	  THE	  AVERAGE	  CO2	  CONCENTRATION	  IN	  THE	  ON	  AND	  OFF	  CONDITIONS	  IN	  THE	  CLASSROOMS	  WITH	  
RETROFITS	  ARE	  SHOWN.
